401 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Strome v. Lane County Bd. of Comm'rs


A136520


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


230 Ore. App. 190; 213 P.3d 1269; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 1103


June 9, 2008, Argued and submitted 
August 5, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Strome v. Lane County Bd. of Comm'rs, 347 Ore. 349, 222 P.3d 30, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 1137 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Lane County Circuit Court. 160621813. Douglas S. Mitchell, Judge.
Strome v. Lane County, 219 Ore. App. 519, 183 P.3d 237, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 576 (2008)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Lane County Circuit Court, Oregon, sustained a final order by respondent Lane County Board of County Commissioners (county) that legalized a county road and denied appellant landowner compensation under Or. Rev. Stat. § 368.211. The landowner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The landowner argued that the reviewing court erred in concluding that the county's order was supported by substantial evidence and that the order properly construed the applicable law. The appellate court noted that the appeal was from a judicial review of the same county road legalization proceeding and, consequently, under the law of the case doctrine, it did not address any argument that the parties advanced concerning the dispute over the location of the road or where it may or may not have existed at any one time. The appellate court did not reach the landowner's contention that the reviewing court erred in concluding that the county had authority to proceed with the legalization as that issued had already been decided. The reviewing court did not err in concluding that the county decision was constitutional as the county had used and maintained the road as a public road for at least 40 years. Even if the legalization process effected a taking, that would not make it unlawful and would merely give the landowner a compensatory remedy. The record before the county was insufficient to establish a right to compensation under Or. Rev. Stat. § 368.211.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

402 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Vinh Ba Nguyen


A136914


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


229 Ore. App. 719; 212 P.3d 1284; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 1038


March 11, 2009, Argued and submitted, Clackamas High School, Clackamas. 
July 22, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
070444863. Multnomah County Circuit Court. Jerry B. Hodson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Multnomah County Circuit Court (Oregon) of carrying a concealed weapon, brass knuckles. Defendant appealed, arguing that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence taken from his pockets.


OVERVIEW: At 11:45 p.m. on St. Patrick's Day, an officer observed several cars in the parking lot of a closed park and five or six people milling about. He believed that the group had been drinking alcoholic beverages based on observing several beer bottles strewn about 10 feet from the group. Another officer arrived and said that persons in the group had been at a recent shooting. The arresting officer asked the group to sit on the ground and whether they had any weapons. Defendant consented to a search, and the officer found the brass knuckles. The court held that the officer did not have reasonable suspicion to detain defendant under Or. Const. art. I, § 9. The officer did not observe defendant in actual possession of any of the beer cans nor did he smell the odor of an alcoholic beverage on the breath of defendant or any member of the group. There were insufficient circumstances from which it could be reasonably inferred that defendant exercised dominion or control over the containers so as to constitute constructive possession of them. The containers could have been placed by any member of the public. The officer lacked authority to detain defendant, vitiating his consent to the search.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress.

 

403 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Shineovich v. Shineovich


A138013


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


229 Ore. App. 670; 214 P.3d 29; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 1017


October 21, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
July 15, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Shineovich & Kemp, 347 Ore. 365, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 975 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Multnomah County Circuit Court. 070363564. Katherine E. Tennyson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for entry of judgment on first claim for relief and for further proceedings on second claim for relief; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the former partner of respondent mother, sought a declaration that she was a legal parent of two children born to the mother during their same-sex relationship. The Multnomah County Circuit Court, Oregon, dismissed petitioner's claims for failure to state a claim for relief, and petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: Both children were conceived through artificial insemination, and petitioner alleged that she consented. The court of appeals held that the presumption provided in Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.243--that a husband was the parent of a child conceived by artificial insemination--violated Or. Const. art. I, § 20 because it was not available to same-sex couples, who could not marry. The court saw no justification for denying that privilege on the basis of sexual orientation, particularly given that same-sex couples could become legal coparents through adoption. There was no reason to permit heterosexual couples but not same-sex couples to bypass adoption. The appropriate remedy was to extend the statute so that it applied when the same-sex partner of the biological mother consented to the artificial insemination. The court also held that Measure 36 did not limit the legal benefits associated with marriage to heterosexual couples and therefore had no application in the current case. Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.070, which created a presumption as to who was the biological parent of a child, did not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 20.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the case for entry of judgment on first claim for relief and for further proceedings on second claim for relief. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

 

404 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lantzsch


A136096


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


229 Ore. App. 505; 214 P.3d 22; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 1018


December 22, 2008, Submitted 
July 15, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by State v. Lantzsch, 349 Ore. 663, 249 P.3d 1281, 2011 Ore. LEXIS 170 (2011)
Substituted opinion at, On reconsideration by State v. Lantzsch, 2011 Ore. App. LEXIS 1004 (Or. Ct. App., July 20, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Washington County Circuit Court. C070765CR. Steven L. Price, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Washington County Circuit Court (Oregon) of unlawful possession of methamphetamine. The trial court had denied defendant's motion to suppress. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress and defendant was convicted. On appeal, the court vacated and remanded for further fact finding. A traffic infraction led to a car being stopped. The officer initiated an investigation against the driver. That investigation, including the officer's authority to question defendant pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 810.410, concluded when the officer arrested the driver. From that point forward, for purposes of Or. Const. art. I, § 9, the officer was required to have some independent basis for detaining defendant. Given that the officer asked defendant to get out of the car, he did not tell defendant he was free to go, and there was no evidence showing that the officer made defendant aware that he was being approached as part of the officer's investigation of the driver, if defendant believed that he was not free to leave, that belief would have been reasonable. However, the court was unable to determine from the record whether defendant held such a belief because the trial court made no findings regarding defendant's subjective belief that he had been seized. Remand was required.


OUTCOME: The court vacated and remanded. If the trial court found that defendant did not believe that he was the subject of a criminal investigation and was therefore unable to leave, the trial court should reinstate defendant's conviction. If the court determined that defendant did subjectively hold that belief, however, the evidence at issue had to be suppressed.

 

405 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Pardee


A136180


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


229 Ore. App. 598; 215 P.3d 870; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 1024


February 26, 2009, Argued and Submitted 
July 15, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Pardee, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 1129 (Or., Nov. 24, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Lane County Circuit Court. 200702801. Maurice K. Merten, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Lane County Circuit Court (Oregon) of two counts of rape in the first degree, four counts of sodomy in the first degree, two counts of unlawful sexual penetration in the first degree, and three counts of sexual abuse in the first degree. He was sentenced to a total of 400 months' incarceration followed by lifetime post-prison supervision (PPS). Defendant appealed his sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.700(2)(b)(D) to (F) in combination with Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.103(2), which together required the trial court to impose mandatory sentences of 300 months' incarceration with mandatory lifetime PPS for each rape, sodomy, and sexual abuse conviction when the victim was under the age of 12. Defendant argued that, because the penalty for intentional murder--300 months' incarceration without mandatory lifetime PPS--was less severe than the penalty that he received for each of seven counts of which he was convicted, the latter penalties were unconstitutionally disproportionate in violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 16. The court held the sentence was not unconstitutionally disproportionate to his crime because it did not shock the moral sense. A proportionality review did not involve comparing the penalty for one crime with the penalty for another crime, except in cases in which the penalty for a lesser-included offense was greater than the penalty for the greater crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence.

 

406 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Halladay v. Bd. of Parole & Post-Prison Supervision


A138293


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


229 Ore. App. 45; 209 P.3d 854; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 749


January 2, 2009, Submitted 
June 10, 2009, Filed


DISPOSITION:  [***1] OAR 255-030-0026, OAR 255-032-0015, OAR 255-035-0023(1), and OAR 255-040-0025 held valid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.400, petitioner inmate challenged the validity of Or. Admin. R. 255-030-0026, Or. Admin. R. 255-032-0015, Or. Admin. R. 255-035-0023(1), and Or. Admin. R. 255-040-0025 regarding aspects of parole hearings for inmates convicted of murder and aggravated murder.


OVERVIEW: The inmate contended that Or. Admin. R. 255-030-0026, Or. Admin. R. 255-032-0015, Or. Admin. R. 255-035-0023(1), and Or. Admin. R. 255-040-0025 were unconstitutional because they treated individuals convicted of aggravated murder more favorably than individuals convicted of murder. Upon review, the court determined that in the case of convicted murderers who were sentenced to less than 20 years in prison under the version of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115(3)(c) in effect between 1985 and 1989, the inmate's arguments regarding disparate treatment of parole after 20 years were inapposite. For some convicted murderers, the rules did not operate unconstitutionally, even under the inmate's construct. For that reason, the court concluded that the inmate's contention that those rules operated unconstitutionally, though styled as a rule challenge under Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.400, was truly an as-applied challenge that depended on the particular facts of the inmate's case. Accordingly, the court held that the rules were valid and rejected the inmate's rule challenges.


OUTCOME: The court rejected the challenges.

 

407 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Williams


A131518


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


227 Ore. App. 453; 206 P.3d 269; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 273


October 28, 2008, Submitted 
April 15, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Crook County Circuit Court. 05FE0006. Gary S. Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Crook County Circuit Court (Oregon), which convicted him of possession of a controlled substance, under former Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.992 (2003), renumbered as Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.840 (2005).


OVERVIEW: Defendant assigned error to the trial court's denial of his motion to suppress evidence under Or. Const. art. I, § 9 and U.S. Const. amend. IV which was discovered by opening an eyeglass case during an inventory of his car. Defendant argued that the police department's applicable inventory policy was overbroad because it required police to inventory the contents of all closed containers, not just those likely to contain valuables. On appeal, the court found that even if a city's ordinance did not repeal or replace the police department's policy, it was axiomatic that the police department policy was invalid at least to the extent that it conflicted with the ordinance. Thus, the policy provided no basis for the inventory of defendant's car as it conflicted with the city's ordinance and was invalid. The city ordinance unambiguously required opening all closed, unlocked containers. Although a limitation on the scope of inventories could easily have been included, one was not included. Because the ordinance required police to open all closed containers, it was overbroad and thus, the evidence discovered in the inventory should have been suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded for further proceedings.

Bonds v. Farmers Ins. Co.


A134011


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


227 Ore. App. 185; 205 P.3d 45; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 154


September 10, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
April 1, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Bonds v. Farmers Ins. Co. of Or., 347 Ore. 258, 218 P.3d 540, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 681 (2009)
Affirmed by, Remanded by Bonds v. Farmers Ins. Co., 2010 Ore. LEXIS 787 (Or., Oct. 14, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Multnomah County Circuit Court. 051213375. Jerry B. Hodson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Multnomah County Circuit Court, Oregon, ruled that a letter sent by defendant insurer to plaintiff insured, and a subsequent notice, formally instituted arbitration proceedings; therefore the two-year limitation period was tolled, during which the insured had to act in order to preserve his cause of action, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. 742.504(12)(a)(B). The insurer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The insured had an automobile policy with the insurer. The policy contained underinsured (UIM) coverage. The insured was injured in an accident that he alleged was caused by another driver. The insurer sent two letters to the insured. One acknowledged the insured's UIM claim and stated that if there was a disagreement on the liability/damages owed by the underinsured motorist, the insurer consented to submit this matter to binding arbitration. The second letter stated that the insured disagreed about the extent of damages owed. The appellate court was called upon to decide whether the insurer formally instituted arbitration proceedings, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 742.504(12)(a)(B), by sending to the letters to the insured. In sum, the insurer's letters to the insured did not formally institute arbitration proceedings. Nevertheless, because the insured did not perform any of the acts that could have tolled the running of the two-year limitation period for filing a UIM claim against the insurer, the trial court erred in granting the insured the relief that he sought.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed, and the matter was remanded.

Cogan v. City of Beaverton


A139820


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


226 Ore. App. 381; 203 P.3d 303; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 126


October 24, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
March 4, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Leupold & Stevens, Inc. v. City of Beaverton, 226 Ore. App. 374, 203 P.3d 309, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 123 (Or. Ct. App., Mar. 4, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
2008040, 2008041.
Leupold & Stevens, Inc. v. City of Beaverton, 206 Ore. App. 368, 138 P.3d 23, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 828 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought review of a decision of the Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA) which reversed the city's decision denying respondent property owner's petition for a minor boundary change. On review, the city contended that LUBA did not properly construe the term "area of land" as used in 2005, Or. Laws 844, § 6 (SB 887) and erred in concluding that the petition for a minor boundary change was not subject to claim preclusion.


OVERVIEW: The property owner's facility was within the urban growth boundary of the Metropolitan Service District (Metro). The city adopted Beaverton, Or., Ordinance 4350, in which, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 222.750, it annexed five contiguous tax lots owned by the property owner without the property owner's consent, four of which were zoned for industrial use and one for residential use. The property owner submitted a petition for a minor boundary change, requesting the withdrawal of only the four industrial-zoned lots from the city. The city denied the petition. LUBA reversed that order concluding that 2005, Or. Laws 844, § 6 (SB 887) had the legal effect of prohibiting the city's annexation of the property. As observed by LUBA, the city engaged in an evaluation and concluded that its comprehensive plan was consistent with denial of the property owner's petition for a boundary change. The city's denial qualified as a land use decision and, therefore, pursuant to ORS 197.015(10)(a)(A), LUBA had exclusive jurisdiction. The court held that it was of no moment that the business in question may own additional property that did not qualify for exemption under § 6.


OUTCOME: The decision of LUBA was affirmed.

 

410 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Miller


A134970


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


226 Ore. App. 314; 203 P.3d 319; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 122


August 27, 2008, Submitted 
March 4, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
D064475M. Washington County Circuit Court. Steven L. Price, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Washington County Circuit Court (Oregon) that convicted him of disorderly conduct in the second degree under Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025, arguing that the trial court erred in denying his motion for a judgment of acquittal because the evidence was insufficient to prove that he engaged in fighting or in violent, tumultuous, or threatening behavior.


OVERVIEW: As defendant walked past a gas station, he kicked a sandwich-board-style "Help Wanted" sign off of the sidewalk and into the middle the road. When the station owner approached defendant, defendant turned toward him, made a fist, and repeatedly punched his fist into his other hand. Feeling threatened, the station owner backed away and called the police. Defendant was charged with two counts of disorderly conduct, one for kicking the sign and the other for his threatening gestures. At trial, the trial court granted defendant's motion for acquittal on the charge based upon the threatening gestures but denied the motion regarding the remaining charge. On review, the court rejected defendant's contention that his conduct was not a proscribed physical act of aggression because it was not directed against any person. Defendant's act of kicking the sign constituted the use of physical force; he kicked the sign hard enough that it landed in the middle of the road, demonstrating the use of strength and power. It was not merely incidental conduct; defendant did not kick the sign to gain someone's attention but kicked the sign because he was frustrated that it blocked the sidewalk.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed.

 

411 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Cyrus v. Bd. of County Comm'rs


A133381


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


226 Ore. App. 1; 202 P.3d 274; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 82


August 13, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
February 18, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Deschutes County Circuit Court. 05CV0511MA, 05CV0560ST. Stephen N. Tiktin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Deschutes County Circuit Court, Oregon, affirmed the finding of respondent Deschutes County Board of Commissioners (Board) approving a claim filed by respondent electric cooperative under Ballot Measure 37, Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.352 (2005). The Board waived the land use regulations. Petitioners, landowners and trust, petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: While petitioners' appeal was pending, the voters passed Ballot Measure 49, which amended Measure 37 and added provisions that altered the claims and remedies available to landowners whose property values were adversely affected by land use regulations, Or. Rev. Stat. § 195.305. The cooperative argued that Measure 49 rendered the appeal moot. The appellate court determined that the cooperative and petitioners would appear to have, at the very lease, the ability to seek declaratory or injunctive relief concerning the cooperative's rights under Measure 49. There was nothing in Measure 49 that would prevent the cooperative or petitioners from litigating the validity of the Measure 37 waiver as part of a separate proceeding to determine the cooperative's rights under Measure 49, whether that proceeding was at the local level or in a circuit court. The parties would not be precluded from again litigating its validity in a subsequent proceeding to establish the cooperative's rights under Measure 49. A determination by the appellate court as to the validity of the underlying waiver would not conclusively resolve the merits of any claim to just compensation under Measure 49.


OUTCOME: The appeal was dismissed as moot.

State v. Muyingo


A118291


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


225 Ore. App. 156; 200 P.3d 601; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 9


October 28, 2008, Submitted on Remand 
January 7, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Adhered to, Clarified by, On reconsideration by State v. Muyingo, 226 Ore. App. 327, 203 P.3d 365, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 129 (2009)
Review denied by State v. Muyingo, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 331 (Or., July 9, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Multnomah County Circuit Court. 951038342. On remand from the Oregon Supreme Court, State v. Muyingo, 345 Ore. 316, 195 P.3d 63 (2008). Julie E. Frantz, Judge.
State v. Muyingo, 345 Ore. 316, 195 P.3d 63, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 862 (2008)
State v. Muyingo, 197 Ore. App. 320, 105 P.3d 899, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 99 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was originally convicted in the trial court on 29 counts of various sex crimes. Several convictions were reversed on appeal and the case was remanded for resentencing, at which time the trial court increased the sentence on one of the counts. Defendant again appealed, and the court accepted one of the three grounds for the appeal. However, the Supreme Court of Oregon reversed and remanded to the court to consider the other grounds.


OVERVIEW: During the first appeal, count 19, a charge of first-degree criminal mistreatment under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.205, was affirmed. On the remand for resentencing, the trial court changed the sentence for count 19 from a consecutive 32-month term in prison to a consecutive 59 months in prison, with 36 months post-prison supervision. The upward departure sentence was based on facts found by the trial court, which constituted plain error under Blakely v. Washington. The court declined to exercise its discretion under Ailes v. Portland Meadows, Inc. to correct the error because, given the circumstances of defendant's crimes, a remand for resentencing would be gratuitous because the trial court could and would lawfully impose the same total sentence. The court considered and rejected defendant's argument that because count 19 had been affirmed in the first appeal, the trial court was not entitled to change the sentence on remand. Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.222(5) (2001), in the event of an error requiring resentencing, the appellate court is required to remand the entire case for resentencing, and the sentencing court could impose a new sentence for any conviction in the remanded case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's resentencing of defendant.

State v. Moyer


A128796 (Control), A128797, A128798


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


225 Ore. App. 81; 200 P.3d 619; 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 16


May 29, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
January 7, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by State v. Moyer, 346 Ore. 157, 206 P.3d 191, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 311 (2009)
Affirmed by, Remanded by State v. Moyer, 2010 Ore. LEXIS 277 (Or., Apr. 29, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Multnomah County Circuit Court. 040935104, 040935105, 040935106. John A. Wittmayer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent political campaign contributors were charged by appellant State with violating Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.402 (2003) lying about the source of their contributions. The Multnomah County Circuit Court, Oregon, allowed the contributors' demurrer on the ground that the statute violated both state and federal constitutional guarantees of freedom of expression. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the State contended the trial court erred in concluding that Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.402 (2003) was unconstitutional. The Oregon Supreme Court agreed, holding that § 260.402 passed muster as a Robertson second-category statute that was not unconstitutionally overbroad. In the alternative, if it was a Robertson first-category statute, it nevertheless was wholly contained within a well-established historical exception. In either case, the trial court erred in ruling that § 260.402 violated Or. Const. art. I, § 8. As the U.S. Supreme Court had held that a law that required disclosure of a contributor's identity did not offend U.S. Const. amend. I guarantees, the Oregon Supreme Court could not see how a law that merely required that such disclosures be truthful did. The Oregon Supreme Court concluded that the trial court also erred in ruling that § 260.402 violated U.S. Const. amend. I.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

414 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Mortg. Galeria, LLC v. Empl. Dep't


A133678


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


224 Ore. App. 370; 197 P.3d 1146; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 1756; Unemployment Ins. Rep. (CCH) P8910


May 14, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
December 10, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Employment Division - Tax Section. T70823.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an unemployment insurance tax assessment case, respondent Employment Department determined that petitioner employer owed unpaid taxes. The employer asked for a hearing on that determination, but the department declined to hold one because employer had failed to file a valid hearing request by the statutory deadline. The employer sought judicial review of the department's final order rejecting its request for a hearing.


OVERVIEW: The department mailed a notice of jeopardy tax assessment, citing the employer's failure to pay unemployment taxes. One of the employer's owners testified that he understood a department employee had indicated that the employer's request for a hearing was valid and a bond was not required. On appeal, the employer argued that it was not required to satisfy the bond requirement. The employer contended that the administrative law judge (ALJ) erred in rejecting that the department granted the employer an open-ended extension of time within which to supply a deposit or bond pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 657.683(1). The appellate court agreed with the department. The ALJ found that the employer failed to demonstrate that an extension or waiver was actually granted. Substantial evidence existed to support that finding. Further, events that took place after the hearing request were irrelevant. The hearing request was invalid, and subsequent events did not render the invalidity moot. The applicable statutes plainly provided the employer the opportunity to challenge the propriety of the department's notice of assessment at a hearing.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the order.

 

415 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ward


A133855


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


224 Ore. App. 421; 198 P.3d 443; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 1751


August 27, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
December 10, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Or. v. Ward, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 517 (Or., Mar. 26, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Multnomah County Circuit Court. 060749177. Thomas M. Ryan, Judge pro tempore.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Multnomah County Circuit Court, Oregon, convicted defendant for possession of a loaded firearm in a public place, Portland, Or., City Code (PCC) 14A.060.010. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in denying his motion in arrest of judgment. He argued that, under state law, local governments could not prohibit a person from carrying a loaded firearm within a car on a public street. The appellate court concluded that the text and context of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.173 unambiguously demonstrated that local governments were permitted to regulate the possession of loaded firearms on their streets and highways, regardless of whether those firearms were kept in a place to which the general public did not have access. Nothing in § 166.173 or Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.015(10) supported defendant's contention that a loaded firearm, when carried in a public place, was outside the scope of local regulation merely because it was kept in a place to which the general public lacked access. When the legislature enacted § 166.173, it intended to allow local governments to regulate the possession of loaded firearms on their streets and highways, even if such a firearm was kept in a place to which the general public had no access. Because PCC 14A.060.010 was consistent with Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.173, defendant's motion in arrest for judgment was denied.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

416 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Young v. Crook County


A139342


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


224 Ore. App. 1; 197 P.3d 48; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 1711


August 20, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
November 19, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
2007250. Land Use Board of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of a Land Use Board of Appeals (Oregon) decision remanding to a county court its decision approving a condition use permit application. Petitioner challenged the portion of the decision rejecting his argument that respondent county violated Or. Rev. Stat. § 215.422(1)(c) and Crook County, Or., Code § 18.172.050 by charging an excessive filing fee for the appeal of the conditional use approval.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner asserted that the assessment of an appeal fee violated both the ordinance and the statute and that the Board erroneously shifted the burden of producing evidence concerning the reasonableness of the fee from the county to petitioner. The court found that although in previous appeals petitioner complained that the appeal fee was excessive, he never made an evidentiary showing to support that argument, despite opportunities to do so. Petitioner also could have made a public records request to inspect the documents that formed the basis of the order that set the fee. However, he failed to avail himself of any opportunities to buttress his assertions with supporting evidence. In addition, the record did not disclose that petitioner made any effort to obtain evidence about the reasonableness of the fee from county officials. Petitioner's argument reduced to an assertion that the Board erred in allocating to petitioner the burden of producing evidence concerning the reasonableness of the fee. The court concluded that the Board did not so err and petitioner failed to meet his burden to establish a prima facie case that the fee violated either the ordinance or the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Board's decision.

 

417 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Nguyen


A133428


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


223 Ore. App. 286; 196 P.3d 40; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 1630


July 31, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
October 29, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Washington County Circuit Court. D062113T. Rick Knapp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Washington County Circuit Court (Oregon) convicting him of misdemeanor driving while suspended in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 811.182.


OVERVIEW: An officer stopped defendant's vehicle for failure to display registration plates in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 803.540(1)(b) after he saw that the vehicle lacked a front registration plate. Defendant moved to suppress the evidence gathered after the initial stop, arguing that the officer illegally extended the stop by continuing to investigate him after seeing the license plate propped up behind the windshield. On appeal, the court held that the trial court properly denied the motion because the officer's belief that defendant had committed a traffic violation was objectively reasonable. Based on its text and context, § 803.540(1)(b) required defendant to conspicuously exhibit his registration plate on the foremost part of his vehicle because: (1) "the front" of a vehicle was the foremost part of the vehicle and the registration plate had to be exhibited conspicuously on it; (2) in none of the other provisions of the vehicle code in which the phrase "on the front of" was used could it be construed to mean "inside the front windshield;" and (3) the legislature had expressly prohibited the placement of items on the windshield of a vehicle in Or. Rev. Stat. § 815.220.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed.

Fishermen Against Irresponsible Reallocation, Inc. v. Fish & Wildlife Comm'n


A135023


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


222 Ore. App. 353; 193 P.3d 1014; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 1296


April 11, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
September 24, 2008, Filed


DISPOSITION:  [***1] OAR 635-006-1015(1)(g)(E) and former OAR 635-005-0055(6)(d) held valid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.400, petitioners challenged the validity of two administrative rules promulgated by respondent Oregon Fish and Wildlife Commission specifying the number of crab pots that could be used by commercial fishing vessels.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners argued that the court should declare Or. Admin. R. 635-006-1015(1)(g)(E) and former Or. Admin. R. 635-005-0055(6)(d) (2007) invalid because the Commission exceeded its statutory authority in adopting these regulations. The court concluded that the Commission acted within the authority delegated to it by the legislature. The crab pot allocation system was consistent with the overarching statutory goal of promoting the equitable utilization of available food fish as pronounced by the legislature in Or. Rev. Stat. § 506.109(3). The legislature expressly determined in Or. Rev. Stat. § 508.921 that the Oregon ocean Dungeness crab fishery was overcapitalized. It directed the Commission to establish, by rule, a system for restricting participation in the fishery with the goal of, among other things, preventing a concentration of fishing effort. That was precisely what the Commission did when it adopted the allocation system at issue in this appeal. Further, other statutory provisions, individually and collectively, complemented - and did not contradict - the Commission's authority to promulgate the regulations challenged by petitioners.


OUTCOME: The rules were valid.

Dodds v. City of W. Linn

CV05030464. Clackamas County Circuit Court. Douglas V. Van Dyk, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sought review of an order from the Clackamas County Circuit Court (Oregon), which dismissed his petition for a writ of review challenging defendant city's removal of an "open space natural area" designation from property.


OVERVIEW: The city acquired a lot and an abandoned house in a tax foreclosure proceeding. The city designated the property as "open space natural area." At the time, plaintiff was mayor of the city. After a new city council and mayor took office, the city council voted to remove the "open space natural area" designation from the property. The property was designated "city-owned." Rev. Stat. § 34.020. The trial court correctly dismissed the writ, albeit for the wrong reason.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

06M72342. Lake Oswego Municipal Court. William Bruce Shepley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Lake Oswego Municipal Court (Oregon), which convicted him of driving under the influence of intoxicants (DUII), Or. Rev. Stat. § 813.010.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a conditional guilty plea to DUII and appealed the denial of his motion to suppress the evidence relating to his DUII arrest. OVERVIEW: The appellate court found that the denial of the protester's motion for summary judgment did not present a reviewable claim of error. Chase v. Blacketter


A131128


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


221 Ore. App. 92; 188 P.3d 427; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 889


October 12, 2007, Submitted 
July 2, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Chase v. Blacketter, 345 Ore. 381, 195 P.3d 911, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 995 (Or., Oct. 15, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CV050608. Umatilla County Circuit Court. James R. Hargreaves, Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant petitioner sought post-conviction relief, arguing that his trial counsel provided constitutionally inadequate assistance in several respects. The Umatilla County Circuit Court, Oregon, denied the request. The petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The petitioner was charged with sodomy in the first degree and sexual abuse in the first degree for conduct involving two victims. The trial court imposed consecutive sentences of 100 months on the sodomy convictions and 75 months on the sexual abuse conviction. The petitioner's trial counsel did not object to the imposition of consecutive sentences. The appellate court wrote to address only the petitioner's contention that trial counsel was inadequate by failing to object to the imposition of consecutive sentences on the ground that the principles announced in Apprendi and Blakely applied to consecutive sentences. The appellate court concluded that the petitioner failed to demonstrate that trial counsel, exercising reasonable professional skill and judgment at the time of sentencing, would have objected to the court's imposition of consecutive sentences on Apprendi and Blakely grounds. The appellate court could not assume that because other trial counsel accurately anticipated the extension of Apprendi and Blakely to consecutive sentencing that any counsel exercising reasonable professional skill and judgment would have done the same.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. McGuire

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Deschutes County Circuit Court (Oregon), which permanent revoked her driving privileges pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 809.235(1)(b) (2003) after she was convicted for a third time of driving under the influence of intoxicants (DUII).

OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Crop v. Crop


A134822


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


220 Ore. App. 592; 188 P.3d 364; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 881


March 10, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
June 25, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
C064484CV. Washington County Circuit Court. Keith R. Raines, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband appealed the issuance by the Washington County Circuit Court, Oregon, of a stalking protective order (SPO) obtained by respondent wife pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.735.


OVERVIEW: The husband argued that the SPO impermissibly interfered with his freedom of expression in violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 8. According to the husband, because the content of text messages he sent his estranged wife did not include unequivocal threats to do harm to her, the SPO's issuance violated his rights under art. I, § 8. One text message from the husband used the word "delightful." The wife became alarmed because her boyfriend, who had sexual intercourse with her the previous day at her residence with the window open, had commented to her on "how delightful the afternoon was." Another text message used the word "time out." According to the wife, this text message referred to a conversation she had with her boyfriend and her daughter the previous day in which she said she needed to take a time out. The court concluded that the evidence demonstrated that the husband had spied on the wife and lurked near her residence while she was inside. When the text messages were considered in context with those actions, the husband's conduct amounted to more than expression protected under art. I, § 8.


OUTCOME: The SPO was affirmed.

030432183. Multnomah County Circuit Court. Janice R. Wilson, Judge.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury of the Multnomah County Circuit Court, Oregon, found defendant guilty of criminal mistreatment in the first degree under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.205(1)(a). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with criminal mistreatment after taking his son to the hospital for burns, including one charge for unlawfully and knowingly withholding necessary and adequate medical attention by lying to medical personnel about the cause of the child's facial burn. The court thus rejected the state's position that defendant's withholding of information constituted withholding of necessary and adequate medical attention within the meaning of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.205(1)(a), in that defendant impeded the ability of medical personnel to diagnose child abuse and therefore to treat the child by protecting him from a risk of future injury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction based on lying to medical personnel and remanded the case for resentencing.

Martinez v. Kulongoski


A130818


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


220 Ore. App. 142; 185 P.3d 498; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 723


October 19, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
May 21, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Martinez v. Kulongoski, 345 Ore. 415, 197 P.3d 1104, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 1239 (Or., Nov. 5, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
05C11023. Marion County Circuit Court. Joseph C. Guimond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs brought an action for declaratory relief against defendants, the State of Oregon and its Governor, seeking a declaration that 2004 Ballot Measure 36, a voter-initiated measure, which prohibited recognition of same-sex marriages, was unconstitutional. On cross-motions for summary judgment, the Marion County Circuit Court, Oregon, entered judgment in favor of defendants. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs alleged that the measure, which the voters enacted as Or. Const. art. XV, § 5a, embodied a voter-initiated revision (as opposed to amendment) of the Oregon Constitution in violation of Or. Const. art. XVII, § 2, and that the measure violated the "separate vote" requirement of Or. Const. art. XVII, § 1. The court concluded that the measure did not revise the Oregon Constitution. The constitutional changes wrought by the measure were not so fundamental and far reaching as to amount to a constitutional revision. The court also concluded that the measure did not violate the separate vote requirement of Or. Const. art. XVII, § 1. The court agreed with the State that the measure did only one thing: restrict marriage in Oregon to opposite-sex couples. Any incidental alterations made to the Oregon Constitution by the enactment of the measure were simply those necessary to effectuate that single, simple objective: the elimination of constitutional protections for, and authorizations of, same-sex marriage. The changes that the measure made to the Oregon Constitution were "closely related" for purposes of Or. Const. art. XVII, § 1.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Shotgun Creek Ranch, LLC v. Crook County


A132214


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


219 Ore. App. 375; 182 P.3d 312; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 508



April 16, 2008, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
02CV0061. Gary S. Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county legalized a road that traversed plaintiff ranch's property under Or. Rev. Stat. § 368.201. The ranch petitioned for a writ of review. An Oregon circuit court affirmed the county's decision. The ranch appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that, under the legalization statutes, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 368.201, 368.001, 368.216, the county lawfully exercised its statutory authority in issuing the order establishing the road as a county road running along the south side of a particular river. The court held that the undisputed existence of a county road was not a prerequisite to the initiation of a county governing body's legalization process, but rather the county governing body had the statutory authority to determine that the road it was legalizing was, in fact, a county road, as part of the legalization process itself.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Goodall

COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


219 Ore. App. 325; 183 P.3d 199; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 511

04C50659. Marion County Circuit Court. Dennis J. Graves, Judge.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion County Circuit Court (Oregon) denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained as a result of the warrantless search of his home under Or. Const. art. Woodroffe v. Bd. of Parole & Post-Prison Supervision


A135825


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


219 Ore. App. 87; 182 P.3d 202; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 356



March 26, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Woodroffe v. Bd. of Parole & Post-Prison Supervision, 190 P.3d 380, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 608 (Or., 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Board of Parole and Post-Prison Supervision. On respondent's motion to dismiss filed July 2, 2007, and petitioner's response to motion to dismiss filed August 16, 2007.



DISPOSITION: Motion to dismiss granted; petition for judicial review dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought judicial review of an order of respondent Board of Parole and Post-Prison Supervision that denied his request for administrative review of an action deferring his release date for 24 months. The Board moved to dismiss the petition, asserting that the court lacked jurisdiction because the inmate failed to exhaust his administrative remedies.


OVERVIEW: Or. Admin. R. 255-080-0005(2)(b) was consistent with Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.335(1)(b) where it provided that a request for review was timely if the United States Postal Service cancellation stamp was on or before the forty-fifth day, even if the request was not actually received until later. Additionally, the Oregon Department of Correction had complied with Or. Admin. R. 291-131-0015(5) by processing the inmate's request within two days of receipt. Or. Admin. R. 255-080-0005(2)(b) and Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.335(1)(b) did not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 20, as neither created a true class. Rather, the statute applied to all persons subject to the Board's jurisdiction, and all persons subject to jurisdiction had to exhaust administrative remedies. The statute and rule did not violate the Equal Protection Clause as using the postage cancellation date reasonably accommodated the government's legitimate interests in having reasonably ascertainable and documented mailing dates and the interests of inmates. The rule did not violate due process where the inmate failed to identify any meaningful risk of error that would have resulted from the continued use of the rule.


OUTCOME: The Board's motion to dismiss the appeal was granted. The petition for judicial review was dismissed.

 

432 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rich


A130233


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


218 Ore. App. 642; 180 P.3d 744; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 324


November 14, 2007, Argued and submitted, Newberg High School, Newberg. 
March 19, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
V0500057. Benton County Circuit Court. Erik C. Larsen, Judge pro tempore.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, an attorney, was charged with disorderly conduct for intentionally causing public inconvenience, annoyance, or alarm by making unreasonable noise, Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025(1)(b), during an argument with a police officer in a courthouse. The Benton County Circuit Court, Oregon, convicted defendant of the charge. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that prosecuting him for violating Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025(1)(b) violated the free expression guarantee of Or. Const. art. I, § 8. The instant court concluded that the trial court necessarily found that the disorderly conduct statute was applied against defendant because of the noncommunicative elements of his speech. That enforcement did not violate defendant's rights under Or. Const. art. I, § 8. The outcome of this case depended on whether defendant was arrested and convicted because of the volume, duration, place, or manner of his words, or because his words were obscene, offensive, annoying, alarming, or the like. At trial, the State presented witnesses who overheard defendant's speech, and their testimony referred to its volume, its location, and other noncontent elements. No witness testified that he or she was alarmed, inconvenienced, or annoyed by the words that defendant was using; they testified, rather, that the volume created a disturbance. The trial court did not base its ruling on the content of defendant's speech.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

433 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Banks


A123839


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


218 Ore. App. 593; 180 P.3d 726; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 333


December 5, 2007 Submitted on Record and Briefs; February 6, 2008, Resubmitted En Banc  
March 19, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by, Vacated by, Remanded by State v. Banks, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 648 (Or., July 9, 2009)
Later proceeding at State v. Banks, 321 Ore. App. 398, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 1643 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
C030755CR. Washington County Circuit Court. Timothy P. Alexander, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for resentencing; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Washington County Circuit Court (Oregon), which imposed consecutive sentences after he was convicted of first-degree robbery, first-degree burglary, second-degree theft, and menacing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree robbery, first-degree burglary, second-degree theft, and menacing. Roughly six months before the United States Supreme Court decided Blakely, the trial court imposed sentence. The trial court imposed a sentence of 90 months in prison on the robbery conviction and, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.123, a consecutive sentence of 60 months on the burglary conviction. Defendant did not raise any constitutional objection to the trial court's imposition of a consecutive sentence. On appeal, he claimed that the consecutive sentence violated the rule announced in Blakely. The court decided to exercise its discretion to consider and correct the error in imposing the consecutive sentences. The determination of whether, given the totality of the circumstances, multiple offenses arose from the same continuous and uninterrupted course of conduct, § 137.123(2), or whether defendant acted with the requisite willingness to commit more than one criminal offense, § 137.123(5)(a), or whether there was the requisite risk of causing greater or qualitatively different loss, injury, or harm, § 137.123(5)(b), was a matter of fact committed to the trier of fact.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's sentences and remanded the case for resentencing. The remainder of the trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Schmidt v. Archdiocese of Portland


A124850


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


218 Ore. App. 661; 180 P.3d 160; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 336


January 26, 2007, Argued and submitted 
March 19, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Schmidt v. Archdiocese of Portland, 345 Ore. 381, 195 P.3d 911, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 960 (2008)
Reversed by, Remanded by Schmidt v. Mt. Angel Abbey, 347 Ore. 389, 223 P.3d 399, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 1016 (2009)
Related proceeding at Schmidt v. Slader, 2014 Ore. App. LEXIS 731 (Or. Ct. App., May 29, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
020403531. Multnomah County Circuit Court. John A. Wittmayer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff alleged victim sued defendants, a Catholic archdiocese, an abbey, and a former priest, seeking to hold the priest directly liable for his sexual conduct and to hold the archdiocese and the abbey vicariously liable for that priest's and a deceased priest's conduct under the doctrine of respondeat superior. The Multnomah County Circuit Court (Oregon) granted their summary judgment motions. The alleged victim appealed.


OVERVIEW: The alleged victim testified that, while the priest was sitting behind his desk in his office, he questioned the alleged victim, a freshman in high school, about his knowledge of sexuality and reproduction. During that discussion, the alleged victim saw motion under the priest's cassock. The alleged victim did not see whether the priest's hand was touching his skin, did not see the priest's genitals, did not see the priest's hand on his genitals, and did not perceive the priest achieving any form of sexual climax. Also, the priest did not proposition the alleged victim to participate in any way, did not touch him, did not ask him to do the same thing, and did not prevent him from leaving the priest's office. Additionally, the priest's conduct occurred on only one occasion and did not occur as part of a pattern of abuse. Thus, the incident with the priest did not constitute extreme or severe conduct constituting cruelty to the child for the purpose of Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.117(2)(a)(B). Therefore, there was insufficient evidence from which the jury could conclude that the priest's conduct was subject to the extended limitations period in Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.117(1).


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

435 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Loftin


A132948


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


218 Ore. App. 160; 178 P.3d 312; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 166


October 31, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
February 20, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified by, Reconsideration granted by, Adhered to State v. Loftin, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 336 (Or. Ct. App., Apr. 29, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
C053471CR. Washington County Circuit Court. Mark Gardner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Remanded for resentencing; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In the Washington County Circuit Court (Oregon), defendant was convicted of second-degree robbery and second-degree assault. Defendant appealed, contending that the trial court erred in imposing sentences that were partially consecutive to each other on those convictions.


OVERVIEW: Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.123(5), in circumstances involving multiple crimes arising out of a continuous and uninterrupted course of conduct, consecutive sentences were permitted if authorized by certain judicial findings. In State v. Ice, the Oregon Supreme Court held that such judicial factfinding violated the U.S. Constitution's Sixth Amendment. The state argued that State v. Ice was distinguishable from the case at hand because, given the nature of defendant's convictions as "equally serious," the trial court could, as a matter of law, impose a consecutive sentence without engaging in any factfinding. The appellate court disagreed, reasoning that the State's premise was fundamentally flawed in that it assumed that consecutive sentences were authorized unless prohibited by Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.123(5). The opposite was true; in circumstances involving multiple crimes arising out of a continuous and uninterrupted course of conduct, consecutive sentences were prohibited unless authorized by § 137.123(5). In sum, the trial court erroneously imposed a consecutive sentence pursuant to § 137.123(5) based on impermissible judicial factfinding.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded for sentencing; otherwise, the trial court's judgment was affirmed.

State v. Walch


A127510


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


218 Ore. App. 86; 178 P.3d 301; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 160

February 13, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by State v. Walch, 344 Ore. 558, 187 P.3d 219, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 387 (2008)
Affirmed by State v. Walch, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 44 (Or., July 16, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
041108. Clatsop County Circuit Court. Philip L. Nelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Clatsop County Circuit Court (Oregon) convicting him of of kidnapping in the first degree under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.235, robbery in the first degree under Or. Rev. Stat. § 164.415, and assault in the fourth degree under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.160.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for strangling the victim and stealing $ 250 from her. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err by denying defendant's motion for a judgment of acquittal because the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's kidnapping conviction. State v. Ferrara


A126826


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


218 Ore. App. 57; 178 P.3d 250; 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 165


March 30, 2007, Argued and submitted  
February 13, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Ferrara, 344 Ore. 539, 186 P.3d 285, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 370 (2008)
Post-conviction relief denied at Ferrara v. Premo, 258 Ore. App. 682, 311 P.3d 1252, 2013 Ore. App. LEXIS 1175 (2013)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Ferrara v. Taylor, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 145463 (D. Or., Oct. 23, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
200400019. Lane County Circuit Court. Gregory Foote, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Remanded for resentencing; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Lane County Circuit Court (Oregon) of one count of intentional murder with a firearm in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 163.115, 161.610 and two counts of felon in possession of a firearm in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.270. He was sentenced and ordered to pay restitution. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the denial of his suppression motion, as well as his sentence. The court affirmed the convictions, but remanded for resentencing. Or. Rev. Stat. § 146.107 authorized the deputy medical examiner to enter a trailer to examine the body and the examiner had a statutory duty to investigate and certify the cause and manner of the victim's death pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 146.090(1). This did not amount to an unreasonable search and seizure under U.S. Const. amend. IV or Or. Const. art. 1, § 9, given that there was probable cause and exigent circumstances. The trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to suppress. Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.106, restitution was authorized if (1) the murder resulted in pecuniary damages to the victim's minor son, (2) the State presented evidence of the nature and amount of the child's pecuniary damages, and (3) the child suffered pecuniary damages. Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.103(2), the State was required to prove some quantifiable and ascertainable damages that naturally flowed from the victim's death. The State failed to offer any evidence in that regard and the trial court thus erred in ordering restitution.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentences and remanded for resentencing, as the record was insufficient to support the restitution order. Otherwise, the court affirmed.

Rooklidge v. Driver & Motor Vehicle Servs. Branch of the Or. DOT


A122472


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


217 Ore. App. 172; 174 P.3d 1120; 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1849


August 2, 2007, Submitted on Record and Briefs 
December 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Rooklidge v. Dmv, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 4552 (U.S., June 22, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
020909052. Multnomah County Circuit Court. Alicia A. Fuchs, Judge.
Rooklidge v. Driver & Motor Vehicle Servs., 2006 Ore. LEXIS 636 (Or., June 13, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment vacated; remanded to the circuit court with instructions to remand to Department of Transportation, Motor Vehicle Services Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver challenged a decision of the Multnomah County Circuit Court (Oregon), which dismissed respondent insurer as a party and affirmed an order of respondent Driver and Motor Vehicle Services Branch of the Oregon Department of Transportation (DMV), which refused the driver's request for a contested case proceeding.


OVERVIEW: The driver was involved in an automobile accident. The insurer determined that the driver's car was a total loss and notified the DMV accordingly. The DMV instructed the driver to cancel her title and registration. The driver sought a contested case proceeding, which the DMV refused. The trial court affirmed the DMV's order and dismissed the insurer as a party. On appeal, the court vacated the trial court's judgment and remanded the matter back to the DMV. The driver had a right to a contested case proceeding under Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.310(2)(a)(A) because Or. Rev. Stat. § 809.100(1) required a hearing when the transportation department proposed to cancel or refused to renew a vehicle's title or registration, and the letters of the DMV to the driver unequivocally stated that the DMV was cancelling her title and would not renew her registration. The legislature intended the scope of the exception set out in Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.310(2)(b) to be limited to the circumstances when the best way to determine whether or not the person has failed the test is to retake the test, and in this case, there was no test that driver could have retaken.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's judgment and remanded to the DMV.

State v. Crocker

05C46614. Marion County Circuit Court. Joseph V. Ochoa, Judge.
State v. Crocker, 160 Ore. App. 445, 982 P.2d 45, 1999 Ore. App. LEXIS 818 (1999)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Marion County Circuit Court (Oregon), which imposed an upward departure sentence after he was convicted of first-degree sodomy in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.405.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree sodomy of a fellow inmate and defendant currently challenged the imposition of an upward departure sentence. State v. Gallegos


A132618(control); A132619


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


217 Ore. App. 248; 174 P.3d 1086; 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1870


July 31, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
December 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Gallegos, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 505 (Or., June 18, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CF050603, CF060154. Umatilla County Circuit Court. Garry L. Reynolds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Umatilla County Circuit Court (Oregon), which entered a upward departure sentence on a conviction for second-degree burglary entered after a guilty plea.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that his due process rights under the Fourteenth Amendment were violated because a departure sentence was imposed based on the aggravating factor that defendant was "on supervision," which was not specifically listed in Or. Admin. R. 213-008-0002(1). This appeal followed. In affirming, the appellate court rejected the State's argument regarding appealability and reviewability without discussion. Next, it determined that there was no due process violation under the Fourteenth Amendment. Or. Admin. R. 213-008-0002(1) permitted the imposition of departure sentences if substantial and compelling reasons existed to conclude that circumstances other than the elements of the crime itself made the crime somehow worse. In light of the plain meaning of the term "aggravating" and the totality of the context in which it was used, the term satisfied due process because it embodied an imprecise, but comprehensible normative standard. Further, Rule 213-008-0002(1) explicitly stated that the list of departure factors was nonexclusive, and the factor at issue here had long been recognized in Oregon case law. As such, defendant was presumed to know the law.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

State v. Phillips


A128590


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


217 Ore. App. 93; 174 P.3d 1032; 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1812


June 26, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
December 19, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Phillips, 345 Ore. 159, 190 P.3d 380, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 655 (2008)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Decision reached on appeal by Phillips v. Angelozzi, 247 Ore. App. 766, 274 P.3d 315, 2012 Ore. App. LEXIS 113 (2012)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Phillips v. Angelozzi, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 178655 (D. Or., Dec. 20, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
031825BFE. Jackson County Circuit Court. Raymond B. White, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Jackson County Circuit Court (Oregon), which convicted him of robbery in the first degree, in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 164.415; kidnapping in the first degree, in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.235; and kidnapping in the second degree, in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.225.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery and kidnapping after a jury found that he confined a man in a storage shed and then robbed him. The appellate court affirmed. On appeal, defendant assigned error to the admission of evidence that he had participated in an earlier theft from the same victim. The appellate court concluded that the evidence of the earlier theft was relevant to prove the fact that, before either incident, defendant had conceived a plan to steal the victim's property. That fact, in turn, was relevant to the contested issue of whether defendant participated in the robbery. The evidence that defendant participated in the October 2001 theft contributed significantly to the inference that, at least by the end of his 2001 visit to the victim's home, defendant had formulated a general plan to steal from the victim. The existence of that general plan supported the inference that defendant participated in the April 2002 robbery as well. Or. Evid. Code 404(3) was a rule of inclusion; thus, evidence that was relevant to the existence of a general plan was admissible unless defendant explicitly or implicitly conceded the plan's existence, which did not occur.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Stat. § 809.235(1)(b) (2003) was ambiguous but that resorting to the legislative history and other aids to statutory construction made it clear that the trial court did not err in interpreting the statute to require permanent revocation of defendant's driving privileges based on his three prior misdemeanor DUII convictions.

State v. Neill


A126766


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
D041467M. Washington County Circuit Court. Steven Price, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in Washington County Circuit Court (Oregon) of interfering with a peace officer in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b) during police officers' response to a report of a fight in the defendant's apartment. Defendant appealed, assigning error to the trial court's denial of her motion to suppress evidence and her motion for a judgment of acquittal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's son reported that the defendant's boyfriend was with the defendant in her apartment. When the officers arrived without a warrant, the defendant had blood on her shirt and elsewhere, it appeared that she had been crying, and the apartment door was damaged. The defendant said that the boyfriend was not in the apartment and refused to allow the officers to enter to look for him. However, the officers explained that they needed to enter to perform an Or. Rev. Stat. § 133.033 community caretaking welfare check. Despite the officer's ordering the defendant not to enter the bedroom, she dove under the bed and pulled out a pair of shoes. The appellate court concluded that (1) regardless of whether the search may have been initiated unlawfully, the evidence would not be suppressed because the defendant's failure to obey the officer's order posed a threat to safety; and (2) there was no principled distinction between admitting evidence of the defendant's refusal to obey the order and prosecuting the offense on the basis of that evidence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Neretin


A118667


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


216 Ore. App. 453; 173 P.3d 834; 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1700


April 10, 2007, Submitted on Remand 
December 5, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
020342851. Multnomah County Circuit Court. On remand from the Oregon Supreme Court, State v. Neretin, 342 Ore. 473, 155 P.3d 51 (2007). James R. Ellis, Senior Judge. (Judgment). Michael Marcus, Judge. (Amended Judgment).
State v. Neretin, 342 Ore. 473, 155 P.3d 51, 2007 Ore. LEXIS 191 (2007)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of violating, among other things, Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b). Defendant filed a motion in arrest of judgment, arguing that § 162.247(1)(b) was unconstitutionally vague as applied to her. The Multnomah County Circuit Court, Oregon, denied defendant's motion. The instant court reversed defendant's convictions. The Oregon Supreme Court vacated the instant court's decision, and remanded the case for reconsideration.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b) was neither facially overbroad nor facially vague. Defendant argued that the trial court erred in disallowing her demurrer that challenged the constitutionality of § 162.247(1) for overbreadth and vagueness. The instant court affirmed the trial court's judgment. Next, defendant argued that the trial court erred in denying her motion in arrest of judgment because the statute was vague as applied to her. Defendant had not cited a specific provision of the state or federal constitution in support of the assignment of error or otherwise developed her constitutional argument. Therefore, the instant court declined to address the assignment on its merits. Finally, defendant argued that § 162.247(1)(b) violated Or. Const. art. I, § 16 and principles of due process. Defendant did not preserve that argument at trial, nor did she raise it in her appellant's brief in the instant court. Accordingly, because the trial court did not consider the argument, it was unpreserved and the instant court declined to address it, under Or. R. App. P. 5.45.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

 

446 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Roberts


A125584


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


216 Ore. App. 238; 172 P.3d 651; 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1651


January 31, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
November 28, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
210407921. Lane County Circuit Court. Lyle C. Velure, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to driving under the influence of intoxicants (DUII). The Lane County Circuit Court (Oregon) sentenced her to probation and suspended her driving privileges under Or. Rev. Stat. § 809.428(2)(b) for three years. Defendant appealed, arguing that a previous Arizona DUII conviction should not have been counted for the purpose of computing the suspension period.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court improperly used her prior out-of-state DUII conviction to impose a three-year suspension of her driving privileges because for purposes of Or. Rev. Stat. § 809.428--which established schedules of suspension or revocation periods applicable "upon conviction for the offense"--the word "offense" meant Oregon offenses only. Thus, defendant contended that the trial court should have imposed a one-year suspension under Or. Rev. Stat. § 809.428(2)(a). However, the appellate court noted that under its precedents: (1) Or. Rev. Stat. § 813.400(1), which required the suspension of the driving privileges of a person convicted of DUII, applied to both Oregon and out-of-state DUII convictions; (2) because § 813.400(1) gave effect to Or. Rev. Stat. § 809.428, the term "offense" as used in § 809.428 necessarily included out-of-state offenses as well; and (3) specifically, Arizona's misdemeanor DUII statute was substantially similar to Oregon's. Accordingly, the trial court did not err in considering defendant's Arizona DUII conviction for purposes of calculating the length of her license suspension.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

State v. Gonzales-Gutierrez


A125633


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


216 Ore. App. 97; 171 P.3d 384; 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1616


January 25, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
November 7, 2007, Filed

Post-conviction relief denied at Gutierrez v. Nooth, 275 Ore. App. 171, 2015 Ore. App. LEXIS 1488 (2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
030934393. Multnomah County Circuit Court. Edward J. Jones, Judge.
State v. Gonzales-Gutierrez, 209 Ore. App. 170, 146 P.3d 1170, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 1700 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded for entry of judgment of one conviction for attempted aggravated murder as to each victim, and resentencing; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Multnomah County Circuit Court, Oregon, convicted defendant for a murder-for-hire plot that he initiated from the county jail while awaiting trial on separate charges. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in failing to provide contemporaneous interpretations of two English-language recordings that were played at trial. The appellate court found that while Or. Rev. Stat. § 45.275(1)(a) required the court to appoint an interpreter to interpret the proceedings to a defendant, there was no indication in the statute that the legislature intended to require the interpretation of a recording to occur contemporaneously in all instances. The trial court did not violate defendant's constitutional rights by initially playing the English-language recordings at trial without a contemporaneous interpretation. Any error by the trial court in allowing the Spanish-speaking police officer to summarize Spanish-language telephone calls was harmless as the officer's testimony regarding statements made by a person other than defendant or a codefendant had little, if anything, to do with the charges against defendant. Defendant's convictions based on his conduct involving one victim merged into one conviction for attempted aggravated murder and his conduct involving another victim merged into a second conviction for attempted aggravated murder.


OUTCOME: The judgment was vacated and remanded for entry of judgment of one conviction for attempted aggravated murder as to each victim, and resentencing; all other issues were affirmed.

215 Ore. App. 643; 171 P.3d 972; 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1544

0302591CR. Klamath County Circuit Court. Roxanne B. Osborne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Klamath County Circuit Court, Oregon, convicting him of attempted rape in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: When defendant was 16, he had sexual intercourse with an 11-year-old girl. Defendant's counsel and the prosecutor negotiated a plea bargain containing two parts. First, defendant agreed to admit in juvenile court to conduct that, if committed by an adult, would constitute the crime of harassment, for which he would be placed on probation for three years. Second, defendant agreed to be tried as an adult for attempted rape in the first degree and to plead guilty, with the proviso that a conviction would not be entered and he would not be sentenced if he complied with certain conditions of probation. Defendant was found to have violated his probation and was found to have failed to meet the conditions of his plea agreement. The trial court entered the attempted rape conviction that had been held in abeyance and imposed a sentence of 60 months' incarceration plus post-prison supervision. The appellate court ruled that defendant's claims could not be reviewed on appeal from a guilty plea under Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.050. Defendant challenged neither the duration of his sentence nor its constitutionality.


OUTCOME: The appeal was dismissed.
