Liberty Northwest Ins. Corp. v. Or. Ins. Guar. Ass'n


A126364


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


206 Ore. App. 102; 136 P.3d 49; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 662


October 11, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
May 17, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 0307-08018. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. John A. Wittmayer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insurance company appealed the summary judgment by the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon) in favor of defendant Oregon Insurance Guaranty Association (OIGA) in the company's action seeking a declaration that Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 734.695, 734.510, on which OIGA relied to deny the company's subrogation claim against an insolvent insurer violated the remedy guarantee of Or. Const. art. I, § 10.


OVERVIEW: The company was the workers' compensation insurance carrier for a county. When an injured employee whom the company had paid benefits to filed a third party claim against parties insured by the insolvent insurer, OIGA stepped in to defend against the third-party claim and the company sought to assert its statutory lien under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 656.580, 656.593 against OIGA. The court did not agree with OIGA's argument that the case was not ripe on the basis that the company might have a remedy in an insolvency proceeding because the existence of another remedy was irrelevant to the constitutionality of the legislation. Also, the company's failure to preserve the constitutional issue was not an impediment per se because OIGA relied on estoppel as an alternative ground for affirmance without further discussion. However, the court affirmed, and held that upon examination of the remedy clause in context, it determined that the clause applied to natural persons only, but not to artificial entities. Because there was no dispute that the company was not a natural person, it followed that it was not subject of the guarantee of the remedy clause of Or. Const. art. I, § 10.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.


 

489 of 770 DOCUMENTS



Qwest Corp. v. PUC


A123511


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


205 Ore. App. 370; 135 P.3d 321; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 583


October 26, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
May 3, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Qwest Corp. v. PUC, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 1189 (Or., Nov. 21, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Review from Public Utility Commission of Oregon.



DISPOSITION: OAR 860-028-0110(7), 860-028-0130, 860-028-0140, 860-028-0150, 860-028-0160, 860-280-0170, 860-028-0180, and 860-028-0220 held valid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Public Utility Commission of Oregon (PUC) adopted rules regarding penalties for attachments to utility poles by non-owners in the absence of a contractual relationship between the pole user and pole owner. Petitioner corporation challenged the rules under Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.400.


OVERVIEW: The corporation argued that the PUC exceeded its authority when it enacted the rules. Alternatively, it argued that the legislature made an unconstitutional delegation to the PUC under Ore. Const. art. I, § 21, art. III, § 1, and art. IV, §1 and that the PUC's rules violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. The appellate court found that the promulgation of the penalty rules fell within the reach of the legislature's grant of authority to the PUC and it deferred to the PUC's actions so long as they were consistent with the legislature's purpose to promote the establishment and maintenance of safe and efficient utility poles and other applicable laws. Because the sanctions that may be imposed under the rules were designed to deter unauthorized and unsafe attachments on utility poles, the PUC did not exceed its authority by adopting the challenged rules. The rules satisfied the test that the danger of discriminatory action not be outweighed by the practical necessities of the efficient administration of legislative policy. The rules comported with the Oregon Constitution and did not violate the Equal Protection Clause.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Belgarde v. Linn


A126193


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


205 Ore. App. 433; 134 P.3d 1082; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 605


November 22, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
May 3, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Belgarde v. Linn, 341 Ore. 197, 140 P.3d 580, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 757 (Or., July 18, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 0405-05273. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Frank L. Bearden, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed a decision of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which granted the motion for summary judgment by defendants, county commissioners and officials, in the taxpayer's action under Or. Rev. Stat. § 294.100 for declaratory relief, damages, and a permanent injunction prohibiting defendants from expending public funds for the issuance of marriage licenses to same-sex couples.


OVERVIEW: Based on the advice of the county attorney, defendants directed the marriage license division to issue marriage licenses to same-sex couples. In the meantime, nine same-sex couples filed an action seeking a declaration that statutes prohibiting them from marrying violated Or. Const. art. I, § 20. The trial court in that case enjoined the county from issuing marriage licenses to same-sex couples until the legislature had an opportunity to enact remedial legislation. The court affirmed, and held that under recent decisions, public officials could avoid liability if they relied on the advice of counsel. Further, statements contained in affidavits submitted by the taxpayer under Or. R. Civ. P. 47E did not create a triable issue because they were not offered to establish facts, rather they were offered as expert legal opinions about the underlying issues of whether the county could issue marriage licenses to same-sex couples. Also, because it was concluded in the case filed by the same-sex couples that the county lacked authority to issue marriage licences to same-sex couples, the claim for injunctive relief was moot.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

State v. Milburn


A123203


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


205 Ore. App. 205; 134 P.3d 969; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 519


November 16, 2005, Argued and Submitted, Sweet Home High School, Sweet Home.  
April 26, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 03-6220. Appeal from Circuit Court, Tillamook County. Richard W. Roll, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Circuit Court, Tillamook County (Oregon), which convicted him of misdemeanor angling while revoked under Or. Rev. Stat. § 497.441. Specifically, he challenged the denial of his motion for an acquittal.


OVERVIEW: In 2002, defendant's angling privileges were revoked under Or. Rev. Stat. § 497.415(6). Thereafter, he was caught participating in a "free fishing weekend" under Or. Rev. Stat. § 497.079 and Or. Admin. R. 635-011-0102. He was charged with angling without a license under Or. Rev. Stat. § 479.441. Defendant's motion for an acquittal was denied, and he was convicted. In reversing, the appellate court engaged in a statutory construction analysis. At the first level of the analysis, the appellate court determined that Or. Rev. Stat. § 497.079 was unambiguous. The exemption from licensing requirements embodied in that statute was unqualified. Section 497.079 applied to all Oregonians, including those whose angling licenses had been revoked. Defendant, in fishing on "free fishing weekend," was engaging in an angling activity that was exempted from licensing requirements. Consequently, he was not engaging in an "activity" prohibited by, and punishable under, Or. Rev. Stat. § 497.441.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed.

State ex rel. Juvenile Dep't v. Smith (In re Smith)


A128612


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


205 Ore. App. 152; 133 P.3d 924; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 506


December 5, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
April 19, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 8808-823128, 0504-90583. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Nan G. Waller, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of the decision of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which found in favor of respondent, the Department of Human Services (DHS), and granted the DHS's petition seeking a guardian to act as the child's health care representative.


OVERVIEW: The DHS petitioned the circuit court to appoint a guardian for the child, who was a severely disabled three-and-a-half-year-old ward of the court in the custody of the DHS. In particular, the DHS sought a guardian to act as the child's health care representative with authority to decide whether to withhold or withdraw life-sustaining procedures pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 127.635, even over the objection of the child's mother. The circuit court granted the DHS's petition and the court affirmed on appeal pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 125.305(1)(b), stating that there was clear evidence that the appointment was necessary as a means of providing continuing care and supervision of the child. That was based on the mother's non-relationship with the child. She was a stranger to the child and had never asked the DHS caseworker for medical information regarding her child. The State established that the mother was unfit in respect to making the decision to withhold or withdraw life-sustaining procedures.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

493 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Dyrdahl v. DOT


A124857


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


204 Ore. App. 509; 131 P.3d 770; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 282


August 4, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
March 8, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CV03080087. Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Deanne L. Darling, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions to reinstate DMV order suspending driving privileges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Driver and Motor Vehicle Services Division (DMV), appealed a judgment of the Circuit Court, Clackamas County (Oregon) that ordered the DMV to reinstate respondent petitioner's driving privileges after his conviction in Arizona for driving while under the influence of intoxicants (DUII), on which the DMV based the suspension under Or. Rev. Stat. § 813.400, had been set aside following petitioner's completion of a treatment program.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner's driving privileges were suspended pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 813.400(1) based on a DUII conviction in Arizona. The DMV refused to reinstate petitioner's privileges after his Arizona conviction was set aside. The circuit court ordered the DMV to reinstate petitioner's privileges, on the belief that had petitioner committed the DUII in Oregon, he would have been eligible for diversion and avoided conviction and suspension. On appeal, the court held that the circuit court erred because petitioner had been convicted in Arizona, even though it was later set aside; thus the mandatory suspension provisions of Or. Rev. Stat. § 813.400(1) applied, rather than the discretionary provisions of Or. Rev. Stat. § 809.400(1). The court noted that the Driver License Compact, Or. Rev. Stat. § 802.540, required that out-of-state conduct be treated the same as in-state conduct, and that the test for similar treatment was whether the DUII statutes were similar. The court held that the Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 28-1381(A)(1) and Or. Rev. Stat. § 813.010(1)(b), were substantially similar; thus the DMV complied with Or. Rev. Stat. § 813.400(1) when it suspended petitioner's privileges.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the circuit court judgment and remanded the case with instructions to reinstate the DMV order suspending petitioner's driving privileges.

 

494 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Meyrovich


A124680


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


204 Ore. App. 385; 129 P.3d 729; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 199


October 20, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
February 15, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Meyrovich, 340 Ore. 673, 136 P.3d 743, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 506 (2006)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Meyrovich v. Or. Bd. of Parole & Post Prison Supervision, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 56040 (D. Or., Feb. 17, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 03C52549. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Thomas M. Hart, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Marion County Circuit Court, Oregon, of first degree burglary and first degree sexual abuse. Defendant received a sentence of 90 months for the burglary. Because defendant had been convicted of at least two prior felony sex crimes, he received a life sentence on the sexual abuse conviction pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.719(1). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: After gaining access to the victim's dwelling, defendant forcibly kissed her on the neck. Defendant argued, inter alia, the trial court erred in denying his motion for judgment of acquittal on the sexual abuse charge because the victim's neck did not qualify as an "intimate part" under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.305(6). The instant court concluded that a reasonable jury could find beyond a doubt that, when defendant put his mouth on the victim's neck, he knew or should have known that she considered it, under the circumstances, to be an "intimate" part. Immediately before defendant touched the victim's neck, she had told him that she was not interested in dating him, and she had pushed him away when he forcibly embraced her. A reasonable person in defendant's position should have known that the victim would have regarded his touching her neck to be touching an intimate part. The trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for a judgment of acquittal. The instant court could not say that sentencing defendant to life in prison shocked the moral sense of all reasonable people. Defendant had been convicted of nine prior sex offenses, some of which involved the use of force.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

495 of 770 DOCUMENTS



Hamilton v. Lane County


A125189


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


204 Ore. App. 147; 129 P.3d 235; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 142


December 14, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
February 8, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 16-02-20072. Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Darryl L. Larson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sued defendant county and others on discrimination and retaliation claims. The county's motion for directed verdict was denied and it was found liable. The Circuit Court of Lane County, Oregon, denied the employee's request to resubmit the case and awarded her no compensatory damages, but did award her punitive damages. The county's motion for judgment not withstanding the verdict (JNOV) was granted. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the employee contended, inter alia, that the trial court erred in granting the county's motion for JNOV. The appellate court found that the trial court granted the JNOV motion on two grounds (public entity immunity and the necessity of predicate compensatory damages) that the county had not previously asserted in a motion for a directed verdict. The county could have raised its "immunity" ground at the time that it moved for a directed verdict, but failed to do so. Further, although the county could not have raised the irreconcilability of the jury's compensatory and punitive damage determinations before the case was submitted to the jury, the county could not, consistently with Or. R. Civ. P. 63(A), obtain a JNOV on that ground. Rather, the county was limited to seeking to resubmit the matter to the jury with proper instructions pursuant to Or. R. Civ. P. 59(G)(4), or, if that request were denied, to seeking a new trial. The county's failure to move for a new trial in the alternative to its motion for JNOV effected a waiver of the new trial remedy. As such, the trial court erred in granting the county's motion for JNOV.


OUTCOME: The judgment notwithstanding the verdict was reversed, and the case was remanded for the trial court to address the merits of the county's motion to reduce the punitive damages award.


 

496 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Herrera-Lopez


A126010 (Control), A126011 (Cases Consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


204 Ore. App. 188; 129 P.3d 238; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 141


October 31, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
February 8, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Herrera-Lopez, 341 Ore. 140, 139 P.3d 258, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 672 (2006)
Post-conviction relief denied at Herrera-Lopez v. Premo, 268 Ore. App. 717, 344 P.3d 567, 2015 Ore. App. LEXIS 54 (2015)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Herrera-Lopez v. Nooth, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 143998 (D. Or., Oct. 18, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 04C-46803, 04C-47026. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Susan M. Tripp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant pleaded guilty to first degree kidnapping and second degree assault, the Circuit Court of Marion County, Oregon, sentenced him to 90 months' incarceration for the kidnapping and, consecutive to that sentence, 70 months' incarceration for the assault. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended the trial court erred by imposing consecutive sentences based on facts that were not proved to a jury beyond a reasonable doubt or admitted by him, in violation of Apprendi and Blakely. The appellate court disagreed, finding first that defendant pleaded guilty to more than one criminal offense arising out of a continuous and uninterrupted course of conduct; therefore, the sentences were presumptively concurrent under Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.123(4). However, the assault included the actual infliction of physical injury, whereas the kidnapping offense included only moving the victim from one place to another for the purpose of causing physical injury, and defendant's guilty plea and his colloquy with the trial court amounted to admissions of that fact. As such, concurrent sentences were appropriate under Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.123(5)(b) and, under a straightforward application of the Blakely rule, the trial court's sentence was not unconstitutional. Even if Blakely and Apprendi applied to the imposition of consecutive sentences, the trial court did not violate defendant's right to a jury trial as the admitted facts could be used in sentencing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

497 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Easton


A118180


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


204 Ore. App. 1; 126 P.3d 1256; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 53


June 30, 2005, Argued and Submitted; November 17, 2005, Submitted  
January 25, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Easton, 340 Ore. 673, 136 P.3d 743, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 505 (Or., May 23, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] C973377CR. Appeal from Circuit Court, Washington County. Gayle A. Nachtigal, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Circuit Court, Washington County (Oregon), which entered an amended judgment, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.083, and made defendant's 14-month sentence in his first case consecutive to the 60-month sentence in his second case.


OVERVIEW: In two separate cases, defendant pleaded no contest to charges of attempted first-degree sexual abuse and first-degree rape. He received a 60-month sentence for the rape conviction and a 14-month sentence for the attempted sexual abuse conviction. Defendant stipulated, and the trial court ordered at the sentencing proceeding, that the sentences were to be served consecutively. However, the written judgments did not so specify. Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.083, the trial court later entered an amended judgment in the first case to correct the error and make the 14-month sentence in that case consecutive to the 60-month sentence in the second case. Defendant appealed from the amended judgment, but the court affirmed, stating that § 138.083 permitted courts to correct any erroneous term in the judgment. Additionally, the common law rule set forth in the case had no application. The legislature had the ability to modify such common law rules and it clearly did so by enacting § 138.083, which specifically allowed for the type of sentence modification at issue in the case, notwithstanding that the sentence had been executed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Olsen v. Deschutes County


A123197


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


204 Ore. App. 7; 127 P.3d 655; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 54


May 12, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
January 25, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Olsen v. Deschutes County, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 629 (Or., June 13, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 00-CV-0624-MA. Appeal from Circuit Court, Deschutes County. A. Michael Adler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In actions brought by plaintiffs, three former employees of a county respite care facility, against defendant county for negligence, common law wrongful termination, violation of the unlawful employment practices law, and assault, the county appealed a judgment of the Deschutes County Circuit Court, Oregon, following a jury verdict in favor of the former employees.


OVERVIEW: Two of the former employees sued under Or. Rev. Stat. § 659.035, part of the general unlawful employment practices law, for violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 659.510, the public employee whistleblower law. The county argued that the trial court should have granted its motion to dismiss the second employee's whistleblower claim because she brought her claim under § 659.510, which had a 90-day limitations period. The second employee argued that her whistleblower claim was brought under the general unlawful employment practices statute and that Or. Rev. Stat. § 659.121's one-year limitation applied. The instant court concluded that the one-year limitation period of § 659.121 governed. It also concluded that plaintiffs' wrongful termination claims were not legally barred due to the existence of an alternative statutory cause of action, namely the workers' compensation system. Although the county argued that plaintiffs failed to exhaust administrative remedies, plaintiffs were not using the judicial system to challenge the outcome of an administrative process. Therefore, no exhaustion issue existed. The trial court properly denied the county's motion to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Morsman v. City of Madras


A129627


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


203 Ore. App. 546; 126 P.3d 6; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 8


October 10, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
January 4, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Morsman v. City of Madras, 340 Ore. 483, 135 P.3d 318, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 431 (Or., Apr. 25, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 2003-170. Judicial Review from Land Use Board of Appeals.
Morsman v. City of Madras, 196 Ore. App. 67, 100 P.3d 761, 2004 Ore. App. LEXIS 1416 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA) rejected petitioner land owners' constitutional challenges to Or. Rev. Stat. § 222.170(1); petitioners sought review of the decision of LUBA that pertained to respondent city's annexation of 759 acres of land.


OVERVIEW: Much of the annexed area was occupied by an already developed industrial park, the city's sewage treatment plant, its airport, and some residential properties, including petitioners' 60-unit low income mobile home park. Petitioners argued the triple majority statutory scheme was unconstitutional under both the Oregon and United States constitutions because it dispensed, in certain circumstances, with a vote of the electorate on a proposed municipal annexation. At issue was whether Or. Rev. Stat. § 222.111(5) required the legislative body of a city to submit a proposal for annexation to the electors of the territory proposed for annexation and then, in conjunction with Or. Rev. Stat. § 222.170(1), whether it selectively withdrew that right from some electors. The appellate court concluded that petitioners were not members of a suspected class. Because the triple majority annexation method provided in § 222.170(1) did not create a generalized right to vote on municipal annexation decisions, because no such right existed under the Constitution, and because the statutory scheme satisfied the rational basis standard, it did not violate the Fourteenth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the decision.


 

500 of 770 DOCUMENTS



May Trucking Co. v. DOT


A118893 (Control), A121939 (Cases Consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


203 Ore. App. 564; 126 P.3d 695; 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 1


February 1, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
January 4, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 99044, 99706. Judicial Review from Department of Transportation.
May Trucking Co. v. Or. DOT, 388 F.3d 1261, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 23768 (9th Cir. Or., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer sought judicial review of two final orders of the Oregon Department of Transportation (ODOT) that were consolidated on review. Both orders denied petitioner's pursuit of a refund for fuel taxes paid pursuant to the International Fuel Tax Agreement (IFTA), 49 U.S.C.S. § 31705.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was assessed a fuel tax by ODOT pursuant to IFTA, which was a multi-jurisdictional agreement that was intended to encourage cooperation in the administration and collection of motor fuel use tax. Petitioner asserted that Or. Rev. Stat. § 825.555 was an invalid delegation of power by the legislature because it did not adopt the specific international fuel tax agreement that ODOT entered into and because the statute adopted future tax agreements. ODOT did not err in rejecting petitioner's arguments. Or. Rev. Stat. § 825.555 was not an improper delegation of power by the legislature. In addition, petitioner failed to prove that ODOT did not follow the proper procedures in adopting IFTA. IFTA's provisions were not rules, but were simply part of an agreement; moreover, ODOT promulgated Or. Admin. R. 740-200-040, which adopted IFTA. IFTA did not exempt idle time from taxation, and with respect to the idle time issue, the same interpretation of the language of IFTA applied. Finally, petitioner waived its arguments regarding the validity of IFTA through stipulation.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Peralta-Basilio v. Hill


A125997


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


203 Ore. App. 449; 126 P.3d 1; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1669


June 20, 2005, Submitted on Record and Briefs  
December 28, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Peralta-Basilio v. Hill, 340 Ore. 359, 132 P.3d 1056, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 340 (Or., Apr. 4, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 03-08-3017M. Appeal from Circuit Court, Malheur County. James Hargreaves, Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty in the Circuit Court, Malheur County, Oregon, to driving while under the influence of intoxicants, assault in the second degree, and assault in the third degree. The trial court imposed a departure sentence of 36 months in prison with 24 months' supervision. Defendant then filed a petition for post-conviction relief; however, the trial court rejected all of his claims and dismissed the petition. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that because the departure sentence on the third-degree assault conviction was unconstitutional, the trial court should have modified it. The State responded that, because defendant did not assert that claim in his petition for post-conviction relief, it was waived. Defendant also contended that his criminal trial counsel's failure to object to the departure sentence on Apprendi and Blakely grounds amounted to constitutionally inadequate assistance. The appellate court noted that a claim not asserted in the post-conviction petition was deemed waived and that the principles announced in Apprendi and Blakely did not apply retroactively in collateral, post-conviction proceedings. The appellate court held that at the time defendant was sentenced, the law stated that a departure from a presumptive sentence did not run afoul of Apprendi as long as the upward departure was authorized under the sentencing guidelines and did not exceed the statutory maximum. The appellate court concluded that had defendant's counsel objected to defendant's sentence, the objection would have failed, even though that principle of law was later called into question by Blakely.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Orr v. E. Valley Water Dist.


A125101


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


203 Ore. App. 430; 125 P.3d 834; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1670


June 6, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
December 28, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Orr v. E. Valley Water Dist., 340 Ore. 308, 132 P.3d 28, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 228 (Or., Mar. 21, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 03C-17163. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Albin W. Norblad, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owners sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), which dismissed their petition for a writ of review of respondent irrigation district's decision under Or. Rev. Stat. § 545.099 denying the owners' request for exclusion of their lands from the district.


OVERVIEW: The owners all had property located within the boundaries of the irrigation district. The trial court determined that the action was not justiciable because the owners had not shown that the district's denial of their request for exclusion from the district, even if erroneous, had a practical effect on the owners' rights. On appeal, the owners renewed their argument that § 545.099 accorded them an absolute right to have their land removed from the district. The court determined that the statutory right that the owners asserted was one whose denial, by itself, constituted a cognizable injury or practical effect. The court noted that it had never held that a landowner lacked constitutional standing to contest on judicial review a land use decision affecting the landowner's property. Because a judgment on the merits would have a practical effect on the legal status of the owners' property, their claim was constitutionally justiciable. Likewise, because the owners asserted an injury to a statutory right affecting their property, they had standing under Or. Rev. Stat. § 34.040(1). It followed that the trial court erred in granting the district's cross-motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded for further proceedings.

Carey v. Lincoln Loan Co.


A117696


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


203 Ore. App. 399; 125 P.3d 814; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1674


June 27, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
December 28, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Carey v. Lincoln Loan Co., 341 Ore. 449, 143 P.3d 772, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 928 (2006)
Affirmed by, Remanded by Carey v. Lincoln Loan Co., 2007 Ore. LEXIS 346 (Or., Apr. 12, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 9706-04753. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Henry Kantor, Judge.
Carey v. Lincoln Loan Co., 165 Ore. App. 657, 998 P.2d 724, 2000 Ore. App. LEXIS 312 (2000)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff home buyers appealed a decision of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon) on remand, which ruled against the homeowners in their declaratory action challenging the enforceability of several provisions in a land sale contract by which they purchased a house from defendant sellers.


OVERVIEW: When the buyers exercised their option to buy after renting for three years, the contract they signed stated that the property could not be sold without the consent of defendant seller and that the buyers would not pay more than $2,000 on the unpaid balance per year. The court held that it had jurisdiction because Or. Const. art. VII, § 1 was valid. The governor in 1910 had sufficient claim to the office at the time, and Or. Const. art. IV, § 1(2)(d) did not provide that provisions be repealed rather than amended if the full text was not contained. Also, the adoption of Or. Const. art. VII, § 2b validated § 1. The court held that the buyers did not to use unconscionability to obtain affirmative relief because they would be asserting only the rights the contract gave them. The consent provision was clearly unconscionable in that there was no evidence of a commercial need for the restriction; the only effect was to make it difficult for the buyers to sell the house and to give the seller an opportunity to seek a windfall. The limitation on pre-payment was unconscionable because essentially the buyers were unable to sell the house, and it placed the buyers at the seller's mercy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court.

Bailey v. Lampert


A124795


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


203 Ore. App. 45; 125 P.3d 771; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1567


December 14, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
December 7, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Bailey v. Lampert, 340 Ore. 672, 136 P.3d 742, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 557 (2006)
Affirmed by, Remanded by Bailey v. Lampert, 2007 Ore. LEXIS 63 (Or., Feb. 8, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 02-05-2014M. Appeal from Circuit Court, Malheur County. Joseph F. Ceniceros, Senior Judge.
Bailey v. Rae, 339 F.3d 1107, 2003 U.S. App. LEXIS 16546 (9th Cir. Or., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Reversed on appeal; affirmed on cross-appeal; remanded for entry of judgment denying petition for post-conviction relief.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner was convicted of two charges of felon in possession of a firearm, Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.270 (2003), based on his stipulation that he knowingly possessed two assault firearms at a time when he stood convicted of a prior sexual assault felony. After the sexual assault conviction was reversed, petitioner sought relief from the felon in possession conviction. The Circuit Court, Malheur County (Oregon) granted relief, and the State appealed.


OVERVIEW: A federal court reversed the underlying sexual assault felony after determining that it was obtained in violation of petitioner's constitutional discovery rights. Petitioner then filed the post-conviction proceeding. On appeal, the court concluded that the prohibition against firearm possession in § 166.270(1) focused on a person's status as a convicted felon when he or she possessed a firearm, not on the subsequent validity of that status. The Oregon felon-in-possession statute, like the federal law, reflected the desirability of a clear, bright line in respect to gun possession. The change to the person's felon status operated only prospectively, so that after the expungement, pardon, or restoration of civil rights that felony conviction no longer served to impose a firearm disability. Consequently, because petitioner did not obtain federal habeas relief from his sexual assault conviction before he engaged in the conduct that resulted in his two convictions for felon in possession, he was not constitutionally entitled to relief from those convictions. Accordingly, the trial court erred when it granted him post-conviction relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed on appeal, it affirmed on cross-appeal, and it remanded for entry of judgment denying petition for post-conviction relief.

State v. Johnson


A121762


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 478; 123 P.3d 304; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1455


July 5, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
November 9, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Johnson, 340 Ore. 158, 130 P.3d 787, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 168 (Or., Feb. 21, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 01-FE-0615-ST. Appeal from Circuit Court, Deschutes County. Stephen Tiktin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Circuit Court, Deschutes County (Oregon) of attempted aggravated murder, attempted murder, and solicitation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his solicitation conviction. The court of appeals disagreed. Defendant proposed to his friend that she engage in the specific criminal conduct of murdering defendant's wife and daughter. In particular, a factfinder could conclude from the e-mail exchanges and phone conversations (as credibly related by the friend) that: defendant wanted his wife dead so he could avoid the financial consequences of a divorce; he wanted his daughter dead because his sexual involvement with her could "cause trouble"; he thought the friend was able to do the killing; he made overtures concerning that possibility; he described to the friend the house plan (including the location of his wife's and daughter's bedrooms) and access to the house; he suggested a date for the killings; he invited the friend to visit the house and finalize the plans; and, the friend's dealings with defendant led her to conclude that he wanted her to kill them both. That testimony could permit a finder of fact to understand that defendant engaged the friend to kill his wife and daughter.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

506 of 770 DOCUMENTS



Kane v. City of Beaverton


A129166


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 431; 122 P.3d 137; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1411


August 9, 2005, Submitted on Record and Briefs  
November 2, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 2005-018. Judicial Review from Land Use Board of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowners sought review of a decision by the Oregon Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA) that affirmed respondent city's decision to annex certain "island" territory pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 222.750.


OVERVIEW: The landowners argued that § 222.750 unconstitutionally deprived residents and property owners in "island" territories of the fundamental right to vote on annexations. However, the court concluded there was no fundamental right to vote on municipal annexations. It noted that § 222.750 involved a determination of whether, in a particular circumstance, an election would be held; it did not unlawfully impose qualifications on who could participate, once the legislature decided to hold an election. The legislature simply exercised its authority to determine that, in a particular geographic area--namely, where territory was surrounded by the boundaries of a city, or by the boundaries of a city and a stream, bay, lake or other body of water--annexation of that territory could proceed without an election. Everyone who fell within the scope of § 222.750 was treated identically. There was a rational basis for treating "island" territories differently than non-"island" territories. Because there was a rational basis for the differential treatment provided in the statute, the landowners failed to demonstrate that § 222.750 violated their rights under the Fourteenth Amendment.


OUTCOME: LUBA's decision was affirmed.

State v. Lott (In re Lott)


A122880


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 329; 122 P.3d 97; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1373


January 20, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
October 26, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Lott, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 231 (Or., Mar. 21, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 30-03-18791. Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Maurice K. Merten, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon), committing her to the custody of the Mental Health Division after finding that, because of a mental disorder, she was dangerous to others under Or. Rev. Stat. § 426.005(1)(d)(A).


OVERVIEW: The state sought to commit appellant after she fired a gun into the wall that divided her apartment from that of her neighbors. At the commitment hearing, two mental health professionals testified that appellant suffered from a mental disorder and, as a result, posed a danger to others. On appeal of the trial court's order committing appellant to the custody of the Mental Health Division, the court affirmed. The court held that the testimony of mental health experts, appellant's past violent act of firing her gun, and her condition at the time of the hearing constituted clear and convincing evidence that she was a danger to others at the time of the hearing. Two mental health experts testified that appellant still harbored the paranoid fears that led her to fire the gun. At the hearing, appellant stated that her conduct was justified because someone threw something at her door and later someone knocked on her door, and therefore she lacked insight into the danger of firing a gun at the wall of a neighboring apartment. Clear and convincing evidence also showed that appellant would not participate in treatment on a voluntary basis under Or. Rev. Stat. § 426.130(1)(b)(A).


OUTCOME: The order committing appellant to the custody of the Mental Health Division was affirmed.

Johnson v. SAIF Corp.


A123541


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 264; 122 P.3d 66; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1349


June 20, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
October 19, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On reconsideration by, Adhered to Johnson v. SAIF Corp., 205 Ore. App. 41, 132 P.3d 1058, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 388 (2006)
Affirmed by, Remanded by Johnson v. SAIF Corp., 2007 Ore. LEXIS 643 (Or., July 26, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 0207-07157. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Jerome E. LaBarre, Judge.
Johnson v. SAIF Corp. (In re Johnson), 200 Ore. App. 414, 115 P.3d 988, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 796 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Judgment dismissing claim for injunctive relief affirmed; otherwise reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff worker appealed a decision of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County, Oregon granting summary judgment in favor of defendants, a public workers' compensation insurer and its president, in the worker's 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action alleging that defendants deprived him of his right to due process under the Fourteenth Amendment by terminating his permanent total disability (PTD) benefits without a pretermination hearing.


OVERVIEW: The worker was injured on the job an began receiving PTD benefits from the insurer in 1981. In 2001, the insurer reevaluated the worker and determined that he was only entitled to permanent partial disability payments. The decision was affirmed and the worker claimed that he was entitled to a hearing before terminative his PTD benefits. The court reversed in part and held that whether defendants were "persons" within the meaning of § 1983 turned on whether they would be entitled to immunity under the Eleventh Amendment. The court held that defendants were not entitled to Eleventh Amendment immunity and were thus "persons" subject to suit under § 1983. However, although the insurer was not an arm of the state for Eleventh Amendment purposes, defendants acted under color of state law given that the insurer was a creature of the state legislature, it had no private stockholders and its directors were appointed by the governor. Further, the due process claim was not barred by claim preclusion because the worker could not have asserted his constitutional claims before the hearings division. However, for purposes of injunctive relief only the claim was moot.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the claim for injunctive relief and otherwise reversed and remanded.

Stirton v. Trump


A125595


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 252; 121 P.3d 714; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1351


July 1, 2005, Submitted on Record and Briefs  
October 19, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 04-CV-1710CC. Appeal from Circuit Court, Douglas County. Joan G. Seitz, Judge.
Stirton v. Trump, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 897 (Or., Dec. 21, 2004)



DISPOSITION: Award of $ 11,013.00 in costs vacated; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent justice of the peace found probable cause that petitioner animal owner had engaged in animal abuse and ordered that the animals be forfeited. The owner then petitioned for a writ of review. The Circuit Court of Douglas County, Oregon, entered judgment dismissing the petition and awarding the county the costs of boarding the animals pending the forfeiture trial and the writ of review proceeding. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the owner contended the trial court erred in dismissing his petition for writ of review and in awarding costs. The justice of the peace argued that the appellate court should not entertain the owner's jurisdictional argument because it was not preserved in the trial court. The appellate court disagreed, holding that subject matter jurisdiction could be raised at any time. Nonetheless, the justice did possess subject matter jurisdiction over the forfeiture action as the owner was charged with animal neglect under Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.325; thus, under Or. Rev. Stat. § 156.705, the justice of the peace had jurisdiction over those charges. As the justice court was "the court" before which the criminal action was pending on the animal neglect charges, the justice of the peace was authorized under Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.347(1) to order forfeiture of the animals. However, the justice of the peace conceded, and the appellate court accepted the concession, that it was error to award costs to the county, as a nonparty, to the writ of review proceeding.


OUTCOME: The award of costs to the county was vacated; otherwise, the judgment was affirmed.

 

510 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Robison


A123659


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 237; 120 P.3d 1285; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1355


March 4, 2005, Submitted on Record and Briefs  
October 19, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 0309-52392. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Gregg A. Lowe, Judge pro tempore.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted for criminal obstruction as a nuisance under Portland, Or., City Code § 14A.50.030 (2003), in the Circuit Court of Multnomah County, Oregon. She appealed, assigning error to the denial of her motion for a judgment of acquittal. She asserted the ordinance was unconstitutional. She also assigned error to the denial of her motion to require the State to prove its case beyond a reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: The record showed that defendant was seated in a walkway area and that her presence required other pedestrians to walk around her and her friend to get past. When they refused to move, the officer arrested defendant for obstructing the sidewalk. The dispositive issue on appeal was whether the ordinance, Portland, Or., City Code § 14A.50.030 (2003), was preempted by Oregon law regulating the same conduct. In that respect, as to Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025, a similar state law governing the crime of disorderly conduct, the appellate court found it was clear the Oregon Legislature intended to preserve from criminal liability conduct that obstructed vehicular or pedestrian traffic in the absence of proof that the actor either intended to create public inconveniences, annoyance, or alarm, or recklessly created a risk of doing so. The legislature consciously avoided creating a strict liability offense out of concern for constitutionally protected rights of freedom of expression. The ordinance prohibited precisely what the legislature intended to permit per its enactment of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025. Consequently, the ordinance conflicted with state law and was preempted.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was reversed.

 

511 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Haney v. Schiedler


A124188


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 51; 120 P.3d 1225; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1320


August 8, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
October 12, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CV02-1395. Appeal from Circuit Court, Umatilla County. Rudy M. Murgo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to a plea agreement, respondent inmate had stipulated to a sentence with presumptive concurrent 65-month terms of incarceration if his probation were revoked. Later, the Circuit Court of Umatilla County, Oregon, revoked his probation. However, in his postconviction action, the trial court found his sentence exceeded the maximum allowed by law. Appellant, the superintendent of a correctional institution, sought review.


OVERVIEW: In Stroup v. Hill, as in the instant case, the post-conviction petitioner had been sentenced in accordance with a stipulated plea agreement that resulted in imposition of a sentence exceeding the 60-month maximum for Class C felonies. The post-conviction court had dismissed that claim, and the instant court had affirmed, holding in part, that a petitioner could not obtain post-conviction relief on any ground that reasonably could have been raised at trial or on direct appeal unless the petitioner demonstrated that the failure to raise the issue fell within one of several narrowly drawn exceptions. Although Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.222(2)(d) precluded appellate review of the petitioner's sentence in Stroup, it did not follow therefrom that he was precluded from withdrawing his plea made pursuant to the agreement before sentencing in the trial court. As in Stroup, the inmate in the instant case did not allege any other circumstances to show why he reasonably could not have been expected to raise his objection before sentencing. Thus, in the instant case, the trial court's allowance of relief based on Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.530(1)(c) was plain error.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

 

512 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. White


A114793


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 1; 121 P.3d 3; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1326


March 30, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
October 12, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by State v. White, 340 Ore. 157, 130 P.3d 786, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 151 (2006)
Review denied by State v. White, 340 Ore. 673, 136 P.3d 743, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 514 (2006)
Affirmed by State v. White, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 1181 (Or., Nov. 9, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CR0001843. Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Alan R. Jack, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgments of conviction for first-degree kidnapping, fourth-degree assault, second-degree assault, and first-degree burglary vacated; remanded for resentencing pursuant to ORS 138.222(5) with instructions (1) to enter a judgment of conviction for first-degree kidnapping reflecting that defendant was convicted on both theories, (2) to enter a judgment of conviction for second-degree assault, and (3) to enter a judgment for first-degree burglary reflecting that defendant was convicted on both theories; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Circuit Court, Clackamas County (Oregon), which convicted him of two counts of kidnapping in the first degree, one count of assault in the second degree, two counts of assault in the fourth degree, two counts of burglary in the first degree, one count of menacing, and one count of interfering with a peace officer.


OVERVIEW: The charges against defendant all related to his assault on his girlfriend. On appeal, defendant argued that the two convictions for kidnapping should merge, because they represented alternate ways of committing the single crime of kidnapping in the first degree. The court agreed. It noted that the first-degree kidnapping statute, Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.235, was indistinguishable from the state's aggravated murder statute, and that second-degree kidnapping became first-degree kidnapping by "any" of the enumerated means in the statute. The multiple intent factors constituted only different theories under which second-degree kidnapping was subject to enhanced penalties. Thus, only one judgment of conviction for kidnapping was proper. The court next determined that the trial court erred in failing to merge the two convictions for assault in the fourth degree with the conviction for assault in the second degree. Finally, the court decided that a single conviction for burglary more accurately reflected defendant's unlawful entry into his girlfriend's apartment than two convictions. The court was unaware of any evidence that demonstrated that defendant's initial unlawful entry ever ended.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgments of conviction for first-degree kidnapping, fourth-degree assault, second-degree assault, and first-degree burglary. It remanded for resentencing with instructions to enter a judgment of conviction for first-degree kidnapping stating that defendant was convicted on both theories, for second-degree assault, and for first-degree burglary stating that he was convicted on both theories. Otherwise, the court affirmed.

Goddard v. Farmers Ins. Co.


A118750


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 79; 120 P.3d 1260; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1333


April 18, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
October 12, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified by, On reconsideration by, Adhered to Goddard v. Farmers Ins. Co., 203 Ore. App. 744, 126 P.3d 682, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 32 (Or. Ct. App., Jan. 18, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 9005-03204. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Douglas G. Beckman, Judge.
Goddard v. Farmers Ins. Co., 173 Ore. App. 633, 22 P.3d 1224, 2001 Ore. App. LEXIS 586 (2001)



DISPOSITION: On appeal, judgment vacated and remanded with instructions to grant defendant's motion for new trial, limited to punitive damages, unless plaintiff agrees to remittitur of punitive damages to three times compensatory damages award within 28 days of entry of appellate judgment. On cross-appeal, judgment vacated and remanded with instructions to recalculate compensatory damages in accordance with this opinion. Otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the decedent's personal representative (representative), and defendant insurer both sought review of the decision of the Circuit Court of Multnomah County (Oregon), which awarded the representative compensatory damages and punitive damages based on the insurer's handling of a wrongful death claim.


OVERVIEW: The current action involved an auto accident in 1987 in which the decedent died following a collision with a pickup truck driven by a drunk driver (driver). The driver was in a vehicle insured by the insurer. The jury awarded the representative $ 863,274 in compensatory damages and $ 20,718,576 in punitive damages, based on the insurer's conduct in handling the wrongful death claim against the driver and in failing to settle that claim within policy limits. Both parties appealed. The court concluded that the punitive damage award was unconstitutionally excessive and also concluded that the trial court erred in calculating and reducing the compensatory damage award. Further, given the plain meaning of the terms "liability insurance" and "insured against liability" and the pertinent statutory context, it was apparent that the legislature, in enacting Or. Evid. Code 411(1), did not intend that provision to encompass and compel the exclusion from evidence of insurance "pooling agreements." Accordingly, the trial court did not err in admitting the pooling agreement.


OUTCOME: The court vacated and remanded with instructions to grant the insurer's motion for new trial, limited to punitive damages, unless the representative agreed to remittitur of punitive damages to three times the compensatory damages award within 28 days of entry of the appellate judgment. On the representative's appeal, the judgment was vacated and remanded with instructions to recalculate the compensatory damages.


 

514 of 770 DOCUMENTS



Bjurstrom v. Or. Lottery


A124915


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 162; 120 P.3d 1235; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1337; 23 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1015


June 2, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
October 12, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 03C-10887. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Joseph C. Guimond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee appealed a judgment of the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), which granted summary judgment to defendants, an employer and some of its managers. The employee alleged violations of the Oregon Public Employee Whistleblower Law, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 659A.203 to 659A.236, and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Plaintiff claimed that he was terminated in retaliation for making intra-agency complaints on matters of agency policy and practice.


OVERVIEW: Among other things, the employee became irate and hit a table during a human resources (HR) investigation into whether he sent a derogatory email regarding a coworker. The court concluded that none of the statements in retaliation for which the employee claimed he was terminated were protected by state or federal law. The court first determined that the employee's complaints were not within the purview of Or. Rev. Stat. § 659A.203. Even if he was fired because of his complaints and not because of his dishonesty and threatening conduct, the termination was not a violation of the Whistleblower Law. The court concluded that the legislature intended the term "mismanagement" in § 659A.203 to refer to serious agency misconduct having the effect of actually or potentially undermining the agency's ability to fulfill its public mission, and the court found that the employee's comments did not fall within this definition. The court last determined that they trial court did not err in granting the employer's motion for summary judgment on the employee's § 1983 claim. The employee did not voice matters of public concern, and for that reason, he had no cognizable constitutional claim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Lahmann v. Grand Aerie of Fraternal Order of Eagles


A122320


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


202 Ore. App. 123; 121 P.3d 671; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1343


April 8, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
October 12, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Lahmann v. Grand Aerie of Fraternal Order of Eagles, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 593 (Or., June 13, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 99C-17528. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Albin W. Norblad, Judge.
Lahmann v. Grand Aerie of Fraternal Order of Eagles, 180 Ore. App. 420, 43 P.3d 1130, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 531 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant fraternal organization challenged a decision of the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon) on remand, which entered a judgment for plaintiff female auxiliary member in her action for discrimination under the Public Accommodations Act, former Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.670 to 30.685 (1999), seeking declaratory and injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: The organization established ladies' auxiliaries for women but did not allow women to join the governing committees. The trial court held the policy of the organization to exclude women from membership violated the Act. The court affirmed and held that due to the nonselective membership practices, the organization was de facto open to the public. Nothing in the legislative history of the Act implied that its framers intended to exempt such organizations. The court also held that because the complaint challenged on the gender-based admission, the case did not implicate the organization's religious freedom because it was not being compelled to admit atheists. The court could not conclude that Or. Const. art. I, § 26 conferred a right of association that applied to organizations such as the defendant organization. Nothing in the record indicated that discourse about matters of public concern or political importance occupied anything more than an insignificant amount of committee time or resources. The record did not support the conclusion that requiring the organization to evenhandedly consider women for membership would impair the organization's social and charitable efforts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

State v. Schneider


A118922


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


201 Ore. App. 546; 120 P.3d 16; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1223


October 5, 2004, Argued and Submitted; May 16, 2005, Resubmitted En Banc  
September 21, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Adhered to, On reconsideration by State v. Schneider, 204 Ore. App. 710, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 344 (Or. Ct. App., Mar. 22, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] C002925CR. Appeal from Circuit Court, Washington County. Kirsten E. Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Circuit Court, Washington County (Oregon) of assault in the fourth degree, recklessly endangering another person, criminal mischief in the second degree, reckless driving, and driving under the influence of intoxicants, in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 163.160, 163.195, 164.354, 811.140, 813.010. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested after causing a car accident. He was convicted of various offenses and the court affirmed on appeal. The trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to dismiss on speedy trial grounds under Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.747. Approximately six months of the 12-month period of delay was occasioned by a shortage of judges. No one contested the sufficiency of the record to support the trial court's decision to delay the trial for that reason and the court found that the total delay was not unreasonable. Because there was no evidence that defendant requested to consult with counsel, for purposes of Or. Const. art. I, § 11, before taking a breath test, the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress. The trial court did not err in overruling defendant's objection to the admissibility of breath test results, and because a witness's testimony pertained to the issue of whether defendant was under the influence of intoxicants, the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motions to strike and for a mistrial. Defendant was not entitled to an instruction under Or. Rev. Stat. § 10.095(7).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

517 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Spring


A122897


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


201 Ore. App. 367; 120 P.3d 1; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1150


April 1, 2005, Submitted on Record and Briefs  
August 31, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Spring, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 453 (Or., Apr. 25, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 0330034C. Appeal from Circuit Court, Benton County. Henry Dickerson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Oregon Division of Child Support (DCS), issued an administrative support order on behalf of respondent mother. Appellant, the subject child's putative father, refused to submit to parentage testing. He sought a declaratory judgment that such testing violated constitutional prohibitions against unreasonable searches and seizures. The Circuit Court of Benton County, Oregon, rejected his request, and he appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the father contended that the deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) test that was required under Or. Rev. Stat. § 416.430(5)(a) was an infringement on his rights under Or. Const. art. I, § 9 and U.S. Const. Amend. IV and that he was entitled to a hearing on the matter of his parentage before being required to submit to the DNA testing. However, the appellate court held that same (whether a blood test or a mouth swab for DNA) was a reasonable search under either constitutional provision for three reasons. First, it was conducted for the purpose of determining the paternity of, and thereby the financial responsibility for, children in Oregon, a purpose other than the enforcement of laws by means of criminal sanctions. Second, it was conducted pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 416.430, the legislative embodiment of the policy enacted by the Oregon Legislature. Third, § 416.430 eliminated the discretion of those entrusted with administering the test; every person who denied paternity and sought a hearing in the circuit court on that issue had to first provide the DCS with a DNA sample. Thus, § 416.430 did not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 9.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State ex rel. City of Powers v. Coos County Airport Dist.


A122358


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


201 Ore. App. 222; 119 P.3d 225; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1068


June 15, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
August 17, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State ex rel. City of Powers v. Coos County Airport Dist., 2006 Ore. LEXIS 729 (Or., July 18, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 03-CV0119. Appeal from Circuit Court, Coos County. Richard L. Barron, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, three cities and several taxpayers, challenged a decision from the Circuit Court, Coos County (Oregon), which dismissed their declaratory action against defendants, an airport district and government officials, that related to the formation of an airport district.


OVERVIEW: After a majority of the voters approved the formation of an airport district, the county issued an formation order. Thereafter, the cities and the taxpayers filed an action challenging the formation. After a motion to dismiss due to lack of subject matter jurisdiction was granted, an appeal was filed. In affirming, the court determined that actions under Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.510 were required to be brought by the appropriate district attorney under Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.610, and not a city attorney. Next, because a writ of review was available as a remedy to the formation of airport districts under Or. Rev. Stat. § 198.785, it was the exclusive remedy under Or. Rev. Stat. § 34.020. The court looked to the three criteria set forth in case law in determining that the formation was a quasi-judicial act. Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 198.800, 198.805 required notice and a public hearing prior to the formation of an airport district. Moreover, the county was compelled to reach a decision regarding the airport district, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 198.785(1). Finally, the process required the application of preexisting criteria to concrete facts, even though a large number of people were affected.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.


 

519 of 770 DOCUMENTS



Salosha, Inc. v. Lane County


A120696


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


201 Ore. App. 138; 117 P.3d 1047; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1024


July 1, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
August 10, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 16-01-14231. Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Darryl L. Larson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee county found that petitioner property owners violated the county code by storing inoperable vehicles and solid waste on private property. The Circuit Court of Lane County, Oregon, affirmed the order. The owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the owners contended that the county's findings were not supported by substantial evidence and that the formula used by the hearings official to calculate the penalty was not applicable to the facts of the instant situation. From a zoning map, the appellate court found that the property in question was zoned as an Exclusive Farm Use Zone and, as such, could not be subdivided into parcels smaller than 25 acres in size under Lane County, Or., Code § 16.212(9)(a). Thus, a failure to comply on this property was considered significant. However, the hearings official made no finding regarding the zoning of the subject property. Even though the appellate court could trace through the record a legitimate path by which the hearings official could have reached his conclusion regarding the significance of the owners' violation, its task on judicial review was not to serve as a pathfinder. Thus, because the order lacked substantial reason, the trial court erred in affirming it.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded.

Schlesinger v. City of Portland


A121555


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


200 Ore. App. 593; 116 P.3d 239; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 860


August 24, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
July 13, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 0206-05898. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Jan G. Wyers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party challenged a decision from the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which dismissed her negligence complaint against defendant city.


OVERVIEW: The injured party was walking on a path in a park when it collapsed. She filed a negligence action against the city, which contended that it failed to construct and maintain the path properly. The city's motion to dismiss based on immunity was granted, and the injured party sought review. In affirming, the court determined that the city was immune under the recreational use statute, Or. Rev. Stat. § 105.682. It rejected the injured party's argument that the application of § 105.682 violated the remedy clause under Or. Const. art. I, § 10. Because a negligence action against the city for injuries received in a public park was not recognized at common law, giving the city the benefit of the immunity provided by Or. Rev. Stat. § 105.682 did not violate the remedy clause. Also, the court declined to consider whether the injured party could have brought an action against the city employees who designed and maintained the park under Or. Rev. Stat. 30.265 because the injured party failed to name any city employees in her complaint. Or. Const. art. III, § 1 and Or. Const. art. VII, § 1, as amended, limited the jurisdiction of the court to the consideration of actual controversies.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

City of Sandy v. Metro


A127336


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


200 Ore. App. 481; 115 P.3d 960; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 804


March 15, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
July 6, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 2004-107, 2004-108, 2004-109. Judicial Review from Land Use Board of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city challenged an ordinance passed by respondent metropolitan service district (Metro), alleging that Metro exceeded its constitutional, statutory, and charter authority. The Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA) (Oregon) ruled in favor of Metro. The city sought judicial review.


OVERVIEW: Metro passed an ordinance amending several provisions of its code, Metro, Or., Code § 3.07.420(A)-(F). The amendment, among other things, moved the city's urban growth boundaries and directed the city to examine its industrial zoning districts and amend them if necessary to conform to Metro's direction. The court determined that the Metro ordinance amendments were authorized by Metro's charter, the charter was authorized by statute, and the statute was authorized by Or. Const. art. XI, § 14. Under its own charter, the constitution, and Or. Rev. Stat. ch. 268, Metro had the authority to enact the disputed ordinance as part of its regional framework plan, even when that ordinance imposed requirements on the city that the city would not choose for itself. Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 268.380 and 268.390 unambiguously conferred authority on Metro to enact ordinances that required the city to enact ordinances bringing its land use regulations into conformity with Metro's. Also, Metro's ordinance amendments were not irreconcilable with the city's freedom to choose its own political form under Or. Const. art. IV, § 1(5) and Or. Const. art. XI, § 2.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed LUBA's decision.

Pinnell v. Palmateer


A113174


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


200 Ore. App. 303; 114 P.3d 515; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 775


December 13, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
June 22, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Pinnell v. Palmateer, 340 Ore. 483, 135 P.3d 318, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 455 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 94C-13573. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Jamese Rhoades, Judge.
State v. Pinnell, 319 Ore. 438, 877 P.2d 635, 1994 Ore. LEXIS 70 (1994)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged a judgment of the Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County (Texas) that denied his request for post-conviction relief from his conviction for aggravated murder and felony murder and the death penalty imposed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimed he received ineffective assistance of counsel. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of post-conviction relief. The court held that petitioner did not show he received ineffective assistance of counsel or that he was prejudiced by his counsel's performance. The court found that his counsel's strategy in conceding petitioner's guilt as to the predicate burglary charge, and thus to the felony murder charge, but contesting the aggravated murder charges, did not amount to a guilty plea and did not require petitioner's consent. The court rejected petitioner's claims as to his counsel's decision not to call an alibi witness because petitioner was not entitled to false testimony. The court found that petitioner failed to preserve his claim of error as to counsel's questioning during voir dire and that he failed to establish any basis for concluding that counsel's questioning was unreasonable. The court found that counsel presented mitigation evidence that petitioner would be unlikely to be dangerous in prison. The court found there was no basis for counsel to raise the diminished capacity, intoxication, or insanity defenses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment that denied petitioner's request for post-conviction relief from his murder conviction and death penalty.

 

523 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Illig-Renn


A114387


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


199 Ore. App. 124; 110 P.3d 137; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 454



April 13, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by State v. Illig-Renn, 339 Ore. 544, 125 P.3d 750, 2005 Ore. LEXIS 685 (2005)
Reversed by, Remanded by State v. Illig-Renn, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 816 (Or., Aug. 24, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CR0014215. Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Eve L. Miller, Judge. On appellant's petition for reconsideration filed January 4, 2005. Opinion filed December 29, 2004. 196 Ore. App. 765, 103 P.3d 1178.
State v. Illig-Renn, 196 Ore. App. 765, 103 P.3d 1178, 2004 Ore. App. LEXIS 1672 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Reconsideration allowed; former opinion modified and adhered to as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Circuit Court, Clackamas County, Oregon, for interfering with a peace officer. Defendant then challenged the facial constitutionality of Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b), but the appellate court found that the statute was constitutional. The Oregon Supreme Court vacated the appellate court's decision and, on remand, the appellate court found that the statute was unconstitutional. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had challenged Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b) on the basis that it was vague and overbroad. After the appellate court issued its initial opinion finding that the statute was constitutional, the Oregon Supreme Court issued an opinion in another case that stated that a disorderly conduct statute was overbroad because it prohibited constitutionally protected conduct or speech. On remand, the appellate court determined that if a statute proscribing disobedience to a lawful order to disperse was overbroad because it criminalized some constitutionally protected conduct, then a statute that criminalized the same conduct plus much more conduct, that is, disobedience to any lawful order, had to also be overbroad. The State argued that Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b) did not explicitly mention any constitutionally protected conduct and was susceptible only to an as-applied challenge. The appellate court held that if speech-neutral statutes could not be challenged facially under Or. Const. art. I, § 8, and that rule extended into the area of free assembly so as to override the logic of the supreme court, it was up to the supreme court to say so.


OUTCOME: The appellate court adhered to its modified judgment.

Jury Serv. Res. Ctr. v. Carson


A122978


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


199 Ore. App. 106; 110 P.3d 594; 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 460; 33 Media L. Rep. 1588


November 23, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
April 13, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Jury Serv. Res. Ctr. v. Carson, 339 Ore. 405, 122 P.3d 63, 2005 Ore. LEXIS 862 (2005)
Reversed by Jury Serv. Res. Ctr. v. De Muniz, 340 Ore. 423, 134 P.3d 948, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 350 (2006)
Vacated by, Remanded by Jury Serv. Res. Ctr. v. De Muniz, 341 Ore. 197, 140 P.3d 580, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 749 (2006)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Jury Serv. Res. Ctr. v. Oregon, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 7544 (U.S., Oct. 10, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 03C-11907. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Charles E. Luukinen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a nonprofit organization interested in auditing a court's jury pool selection process, a citizen, a potential juror, and a convicted murder (challengers), sought review of the denial of their requests the contents of various source lists, master lists, and term lists by defendant judicial officials. The Circuit Court of Marion County (Oregon) granted summary judgment in favor of the officials. The challengers appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the challengers contended the officials' refusal to disclose the requested jury pool records to the challengers violated the Oregon Public Records Law, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 192.410 - 192.505. The appellate court found that Or. Rev. Stat. § 7.130, in combination with Or. Rev. Stat. § 10.215(5) was not a law that specifically provided for disclosure of the records the challengers sought. Master lists, source lists, and term lists were not included in case files; thus, the documents that court personnel had to disclose to "any person" did not include the jury pool information at issue. Nor were the challengers entitled to the requested records under the open courts provision of Or. Const. art. I, § 10. However, the appellate court did find that, as members of the public, the challengers had a right of access to documents relevant to the jury selection process under U.S. Const. amend. 1.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded so that the officials had the opportunity to rebut the presumption in favor of the challengers' access to the lists.
