Baker v. City of Woodburn


A114619


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


190 Ore. App. 445; 79 P.3d 901; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1541


February 10, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
November 13, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Baker v. City of Woodburn, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 285 (Or., Apr. 20, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Pamela L. Abernethy, Judge. 00C-10897.



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified on appeal to delete award of restitution and to remand to city for reconsideration of frontage attributable to Baker and, if necessary, for recalculation of allocation of improvement costs, and affirmed as modified; affirmed on cross-appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a city and a realty company, challenged a decision from the Circuit Court of Marion County (Oregon), which entered summary judgment in favor of respondent owner by reversing and remanding Woodburn, Or., Res. 1554 in an action seeking a combined writ of review and a declaratory judgment. The city was ordered to reimburse the owner for a fee imposed based on a private road improvement. The owner also sought review of the decision.


OVERVIEW: The realty company incurred certain costs when improving a road to serve several stores. The realty company never sought the formation of a local improvement district, which had been contemplated by the city. Later, the city adopted Woodburn, Or., Ordinance 2237 to form a reimbursement district. It also adopted Woodburn, Or., Res. 1554 to create the district in which the stores were located. The owner paid the fee assessed under protest. He later filed an action seeking declaratory relief and a petition for a writ of review. When the trial court found that Res. 1554 was not supported by substantial evidence, the city and the realty company sought review. On appeal, the court determined that the city was permitted to adopt its own method of cost sharing. The procedures set forth for the financing of local improvements were not exclusive; therefore, Woodburn, Or., Ordinance 2237 did not conflict with state law. The court finally determined that Woodburn, Or., Res. 1554 was supported by substantial evidence, based on a public works director's report. However, a remand was necessary because it was unclear whether all of the owner's property should have been included.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified to delete the award of restitution. The case was remanded to the city for reconsideration of the properties included in the fee calculation. As so modified, the rest of the judgment was affirmed.

Sears v. Sears


A117631


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


190 Ore. App. 483; 79 P.3d 359; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1550


May 8, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
November 13, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by, Vacated by, Remanded by Sears v. Sears & Boswell, 337 Ore. 555, 101 P.3d 809, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 911 (2004)
Adhered to, Remanded by, On remand at Sears v. Sears, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 262 (Or. Ct. App., Mar. 16, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 01-2933. Appeal from Circuit Court, Lincoln County. Robert J. Huckleberry, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged a judgment from the Circuit Court of Lincoln County (Oregon), which awarded custody of her son to respondent paternal grandparents.


OVERVIEW: The mother consented to the grandparents' care of her son for several years. When they petitioned for legal custody, she opposed with a petition for sole custody in herself and supervised visitation by the son's father. Custody was awarded to the grandparents and the mother received visitation. The mother appealed, challenging the constitutionality of Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.119, as written and as applied to her. The court found the constitutionality of § 109.119 was not preserved for review and its constitutionality as applied did not need to be addressed because the decision below was inconsistent with § 109.119 and required reversal. The grandparents' argument rested on a showing that they had concerns about the mother's ability to parent, including drug and alcohol use and unsanitary living conditions. The court found these events were shown to have existed in the past, but did not exist when the petition for custody was filed. The court found the grandparents did not adequately rebut the presumption in favor of the mother's custody, as provided under § 109.119(4)(b). Since the mother's counter-petition for custody was not determined below, remand was required.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the award of custody to the grandparents and remanded for a determination of the mother's request for, including sole physical and legal custody of her son and supervised parenting time to her son's father.

Crainic v. Multnomah County Adult Care Home Program


A113907


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


190 Ore. App. 134; 78 P.3d 979; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1857


October 3, 2002, Submitted on Record, Briefs  
October 22, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] 0009-09291. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Douglas G. Beckman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county adult care home program challenged a decision of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which reversed an administrative decision that upheld the imposition of a fine against respondent adult foster care home operator for violating various administrative rules.


OVERVIEW: A monitor for the program conducted an unannounced visit to the operator's facility. She could not find the operator, but was greeted by the cook, who was not a qualified caregiver. The monitor visited a resident's room with the cook. The resident asked for help to use the bathroom, at which time the cook closed the resident's door. Later, the operator appeared and claimed that she had been in the resident's room hiding and taping the monitor's activities. No tape was produced at trial. An administrative decision was issued that found that the operator violated various rules in leaving residents without a qualified caregiver. The trial court reversed the decision. The court reversed. The court first held that it had jurisdiction over the appeal, even though the trial court's decision was issued in two judgments entered on the same day and the notice of appeal had only one judgment attached thereto. The notice of appeal was sufficient under Or. Rev. Stat. § 19.250(1)(c). The court found that a proper inference could be drawn from the evidence that the cook assisted the resident, although she was not a qualified caregiver. Thus, the evidence supported the administrative decision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded and instructed that the administrative decision be reinstated.

 

569 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Voth v. State


A117697


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


190 Ore. App. 154; 78 P.3d 565; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1432


April 3, 2003, Submitted on Record and Briefs  
October 22, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Voth v. State, 336 Ore. 377, 84 P.3d 1081, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 68 (Or., Jan. 28, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 01C-15561. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Thomas T. Hart, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), granted summary judgment to defendant State of Oregon dismissing plaintiff inmate's negligence and intentional infliction of emotional distress claims for noneconomic damages. The trial court ruled under Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.650 that the inmate could not recover noneconomic damages in the absence of establishing economic damages. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The inmate suffered from a medical problem related to his feet. For a time, the inmate received medically prescribed orthopedic shoes from the State. The inmate filed a complaint after the State refused to provide the shoes. The inmate alleged claims in negligence for failing to provide the shoes and for intentional infliction of emotional distress. He also alleged that, as a result of the State's conduct, he was unable to work, unable to receive pay for his work and, ultimately, unable to purchase the necessary footwear from his own funds. The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to the State. The court rejected the inmate's argument that § 30.650 subjected him to civil death. The court stated that § 30.650 recognized implicitly the capacity of an inmate to sue for both economic and noneconomic damages. The statute merely operated to bar the award of noneconomic damages unless the inmate also established that he or she suffered economic damages. Additionally, the court held that Or. Const. art. I, § 10 provided the inmate with no relief because his action was barred at common law and he would have had no remedy due to the State's sovereign immunity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

Landsem Farms, LP v. Marion County


A121871


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


190 Ore. App. 120; 78 P.3d 103; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1362


August 18, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
October 15, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 2002-160. Judicial Review from Land Use Board of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner land owner sought review of an order by the Oregon Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA), which remanded respondent county's dismissal of an application for a conditional use permit. The owner sought to hold gatherings on a private airfield and claimed that Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.015(10)(d) applied to its conditional use application and exempted its gatherings from land use regulation.


OVERVIEW: The property was zoned as high-value farmland, but had a conditional use permit for a private airfield. Petitioner sought an administrative determination that it could expand its airport operation, proposing to hold private gatherings with contests involving dropping objects from aircraft with the intent of striking a target on the ground. LUBA remanded the issue of whether the social gatherings might be "incidental" to activities specified as permissible in Or. Rev. Stat. § 836.616(2)(a), as the owner had not described the nature of the social gatherings in sufficient detail. The court affirmed that conclusion without discussion. The court also affirmed the finding that there was no exemption under Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.015(10)(d) that applied to gatherings "not anticipated to continue for more than 120 hours in any three-month period." Petitioner asserted that phrase referred to multiple events with a total accumulated hour limit, whereas the county contended that it referred to a single event of not more than 120 hours. The court agreed with the county. Given the singular subject, "a mass gathering," the conditions were likely intended to apply to a single gathering.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

571 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Lawson v. Hoke


A117388


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


190 Ore. App. 91; 77 P.3d 1160; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1363


June 23, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
October 15, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Lawson v. Hoke, 336 Ore. 509, 87 P.3d 1136, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 741 (Or., 2004)
Affirmed by, Remanded by Lawson v. Hoke, 339 Ore. 253, 119 P.3d 210, 2005 Ore. LEXIS 516 (Or., Sept. 9, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 0101-00766. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Robert W. Redding, Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment awarding noneconomic damages reversed; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which awarded plaintiff noneconomic damages in a negligence action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was injured when defendant drove through a stop sign at an intersection and collided with plaintiff's car. Plaintiff brought an action against defendant for negligence, seeking economic and noneconomic damages. Defendant asserted as an affirmative defense that Or. Rev. Stat. § 18.592(1) barred plaintiff from recovering noneconomic damages. Plaintiff conceded that she did not have liability insurance at the time of the accident. The trial court granted plaintiff's motion to strike defendant's affirmative defense and denied defendant's motion for summary judgment. The trial court awarded plaintiff noneconomic damages. On appeal, the court explained that § 18.592(1) prescribed an additional factual issue for the trier of fact to decide in motor vehicle cases, namely, whether the plaintiff had liability insurance at the time of the accident. If the trier of fact determined that the plaintiff did not have such coverage, it could not reach the issue of noneconomic damages. Hence, the trial court erred in awarding plaintiff noneconomic damages. Finally, the court held that the removal of the issue of noneconomic damages from the jury did not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 17.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court awarding plaintiff noneconomic damages. The court affirmed the order in all other respects.

Coast Range Conifers, LLC v. State


011423, A117769


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


189 Ore. App. 531; 76 P.3d 1148; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1317


March 12, 2003, Argued  
September 24, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On reconsideration by, Adhered to Coast Range Conifers, LLC v. State, 192 Ore. App. 126, 83 P.3d 966, 2004 Ore. App. LEXIS 114 (2004)
Reversed by Coast Range Conifers, LLC v. State, 2005 Ore. LEXIS 466 (Or., Aug. 11, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Lincoln County. Robert J. Huckleberry, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions to grant plaintiff's motion for summary judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant timber company sought review of the grant of summary judgment by the Circuit Court, Lincoln County (Oregon), in favor of appellee State in regard to an inverse condemnation action. The timber company contended the State took its property without compensation by refusing to permit it to log timberland identified as a nesting site for Bald Eagles.


OVERVIEW: The timber company acquired a 40-acre parcel of forested land. State employees reported seeing two adult bald eagles at a nest site on the land as well as an unoccupied bald eagle nest. The timber company eventually was allowed to harvest no closer than 400 feet from the bald eagle nest and to preserve 50 percent of the live trees between 400 and 500 feet from the nest. Following the annual bald eagle nesting season, the timber company observed that the bald eagle nest on the Beaver Tract apparently was no longer occupied. Its proposal to harvest the timber on the remaining nine acres was denied. The appellate court rejected the argument that the timber company waived the right to assert a takings claim. It also rejected the argument that the whole parcel rule applied. The restriction on the timber company's ability to log the remaining land deprived it of all economically viable use of that parcel. It necessarily followed that the timber company established the requisites of its regulatory takings claim under Or. Const. art. I, § 18. The trial court therefore erred in granting the State's motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and remanded with instructions to grant the timber company's motion for summary judgment.

State v. Davis


A111545


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


189 Ore. App. 436; 76 P.3d 144; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1211


December 2, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
September 10, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 00020414. Appeal from Circuit Court, Linn County. Glen D. Basinger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction for official misconduct reversed; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, an off-duty reserve police officer, appealed a decision of the Circuit Court, Linn County, Oregon, which convicted her of first-degree official misconduct, Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.415, and disorderly conduct, Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025. She assigned as error the trial court's denial of her motions for judgment of acquittal on both counts.


OVERVIEW: Defendant became verbally abusive when she was asked by a train conductor to purchase a ticket. When the conductor offered to arrange for a credit card transaction, defendant became agitated and pulled her police badge out of her pocket and began to fidget with it. When the credit card transaction was refused, she became very agitated, and four police officers boarded the train, asked her to leave, and when she refused, arrested her. The court affirmed the disorderly conduct conviction without discussion. The court agreed with defendant, however, that, as a matter of law, her conduct was not an "unauthorized exercise in official duties" under Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.415(1)(b). The court commented that a jury could have found that defendant withdrew her badge and deliberately handled it so that the conductor would be aware that she was a police officer and that she wanted some form of benefit to flow from that awareness. However, to satisfy the statute, a defendant had to act in an official capacity. A police officer was not catapulted into active duty merely by letting someone see her or his badge while on private time.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction for official misconduct and otherwise affirmed the decision of the trial court.

Eppler v. Bd. of Tax Serv. Exam'rs


A114313


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


189 Ore. App. 216; 75 P.3d 900; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1101



August 13, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 00C-16396. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Joseph C. Guimond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment vacated; remanded with instructions to dismiss case for lack of jurisdiction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff business and individuals appealed the grant of summary judgment by the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), in favor of defendant Oregon Board of Tax Service Examiners. The Board notified the business it was initiating proceedings to determine whether the business engaged in unlicensed tax consultation. The business then sought a declaration that federal law preempted the applicable statutes.


OVERVIEW: The Board sent a notice of a proposed action and a right to a hearing alleging the business prepared or helped prepare personal income tax returns without a license and the individuals falsely held themselves out as licensed tax consultants. The Board moved to dismiss the business's judicial action, as the Oregon Administrative Procedure Act (APA) required the business to raise a preemption argument before the board. Thus, the dispute centered around whether the Board had authority to decide the preemption claim. The appellate court held there was no reason to distinguish the preemption claim from any other issue or defense that the Board had to resolve in the course of deciding whether to impose a civil penalty. The resolution of those issues was necessary to the decision of whether to impose a civil penalty as allowed by Or. Rev. Stat. § 673.730(7). Under the plain language of § 673.730, the Board had the authority to decide the preemption claim. The business could not circumvent the APA by seeking declaratory and injunctive relief in the trial court.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was vacated. The matter was remanded with instructions to dismiss the case for lack of jurisdiction.

Long v. Bd. of Parole & Post-Prison Supervision


A108952


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


189 Ore. App. 56; 73 P.3d 934; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 999


January 4, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
July 30, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Review from Board of Parole and Post-Prison Supervision.



DISPOSITION: Order determining petitioner to be a predatory sex offender reversed and remanded for a new hearing; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner was convicted of five counts of first-degree sex abuse and three counts of using a child in a display of sexually explicit conduct. Based on those convictions, the trial court sentenced him to prison. Before his parole release date, respondent Board of Parole and Post-Prison Supervision (board) ordered that he should be designated as a predatory sex offender. Petitioner sought review of that order.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimed that the board failed to provide him with constitutionally adequate notice. The board notified petitioner that it had made a preliminary decision to designate him as a predatory sex offender because, among other things, he had a history of sex crimes. The board, however, did not tell petitioner, until after it had made its final decision, what sex crimes he allegedly had committed, when those crimes allegedly occurred, or with whom. Without that information or some process that would permit him to discover that information, petitioner was in no position to respond--in writing or otherwise--to the board's determination that he had a history of sex crimes. The same problem inhered in the board's preliminary determination that petitioner used weapons or threats. Because the board failed to provide petitioner with constitutionally adequate notice, its order was reversed. Petitioner argued before the board that a condition of his parole was unconstitutionally vague because it failed to give fair notice of the materials it prohibited. The condition provided a procedural mechanism for removing any doubt about which materials it prohibited.


OUTCOME: The order determining petitioner to be a predatory sex offender was reversed and remanded for a new hearing; the conditions of parole were upheld.

 

576 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Illig-Renn


A114387


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


189 Ore. App. 47; 73 P.3d 307; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1000


March 31, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
July 30, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by, Vacated by, Remanded by State v. Illig-Renn, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 599 (Or., Sept. 8, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Eve L. Miller, Judge. CR0014215.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged, in the Circuit Court, Clackamas County (Oregon), with refusing to obey a lawful order by a peace officer, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b). Defendant demurred to the information, arguing that the statutory phrase "lawful order" was overbroad and facially vague. The trial court allowed the demurrer. The State filed a pretrial appeal, arguing that the statute was neither overbroad nor facially vague.


OVERVIEW: According to the information, defendant refused to step down from a truck when ordered to do so. The trial court greatened defendant's demurrer to the information on the grounds that the statutory phrase "lawful order" was overbroad and facially vague. The appellate court reversed. The appellate court held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b) required compliance only with lawful orders and could not, by its terms, be overbroad. The court also held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. The court noted that in order to succeed on a facial vagueness claim, a party had to show that a statute was unconstitutionally vague in all of its possible applications. As defendant could not meet that standard, or demonstrate why that standard would not apply, the statute was not vague. Additionally, a facial vagueness test to § 162.247(1)(b), had previously been rejected by the appellate court. Finally, the phrase "lawful order" provided a meaningful standard that was reasonably capable of determination.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for trial.

State v. Norris


A114948


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


188 Ore. App. 318; 72 P.3d 103; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 772


May 7, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
June 26, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Norris, 336 Ore. 126, 81 P.3d 709, 2003 Ore. LEXIS 845 (Or., Nov. 18, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CF00-0520. Appeal from Circuit Court, Umatilla County. Ronald J. Pahl, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Sentence vacated and remanded for resentencing; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to one count of felony fourth-degree assault. The Circuit Court, Umatilla County, Oregon, treated defendant's two prior convictions as having merged under the "single judicial proceeding" rule, classified a prior resisting arrest conviction as a "non-person" misdemeanor, and calculated his history score at Level C. The State appealed the calculation of defendant's score.


OVERVIEW: The trial court sentenced defendant and treated his two prior convictions as having merged under the "single judicial proceeding" rule and classified a prior resisting arrest conviction as a "non-person" misdemeanor. The State argued that the trial court erred in calculating defendant's criminal history score. The State claimed that, under amendments to the sentencing guidelines approved in 1993 and 1995, the single judicial proceeding rule no longer applied and prior convictions for resisting arrest were reclassified as "person" misdemeanors. Defendant argued that the amendments to the sentencing guidelines were void, as the legislature did not comply with Or. Const. art. IV, § 22 in amending the statutes. The appellate court held that, while defendant's Article IV, § 22 challenge to the 1993 and 1995 amendments to the sentencing guidelines was timely, those amendments were not invalid because of a failure to comply with the publication requirement of that constitutional provision. As the sentencing guidelines were not "acts" within the definition of § 22, and the 1995 statute that defendant challenged repealed a prior rule as opposed to amending it, the provision did not apply.


OUTCOME: The sentence was vacated, and the matter was remanded for resentencing.

 

579 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wood


A116621


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


188 Ore. App. 89; 69 P.3d 1263; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 668


December 23, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
June 5, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 01021796. Appeal from Circuit Court, Wallowa County. Phillip A. Mendiguren, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged a decision from the Circuit Court, Wallowa County (Oregon), which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence in a drug case.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped for driving without a seatbelt and for having expired tags. After a citation was issued, one of the officers blocked defendant from re-entering his vehicle. At that time, defendant was asked for permission to search the car. After drugs were found inside, defendant was arrested. At trial, defendant's motion to suppress evidence was granted. The State then sought review. In affirming, the court determined that the officers were allowed to request permission to search for contraband under Or. Rev. Stat. § 810.410(3)(e). However, the relevant question was whether, at the time of the request, defendant was being unlawfully detained. The evidence showed that one of the officers prevented defendant from re-entering his car. Under those circumstances, a reasonable person would not have felt free to leave. However, defendant could have still given valid consent even if the detention was unlawful. Because the State never offered any evidence of voluntariness at the trial court level, it was unable to raise the issue on appeal. Therefore, the suppression motion was properly granted.


OUTCOME: The decision to grant defendant's motion to suppress evidence was affirmed.

Rodriguez v. Bd. of Parole & Post-Prison Supervision


A118811


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


187 Ore. App. 282; 67 P.3d 970; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 527



April 17, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Rodriguez v. Bd. of Parole & Post-Prison Supervision, 203 Ore. App. 808, 129 P.3d 803, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 62 (Or. Ct. App., Jan. 18, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Review from Board of Parole and Post-Prison Supervision. On petitioner's motion for leave to proceed filed November 27, 2002, and petitioner's supplemental pro se motion for leave to proceed filed December 17, 2002. On respondent's response to motion for leave to proceed filed December 10, 2002, and respondent's response to supplemental pro se motion for leave to proceed filed December 30, 2002.
Rodriguez v. Oregon State Penitentiary, 81 Ore. App. 107, 724 P.2d 350, 1986 Ore. App. LEXIS 3437 (1986)



DISPOSITION: Motion for leave to proceed allowed; supplemental pro se motion for leave to proceed denied as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought judicial review of respondent Oregon Board of Parole and Post-Prison Supervision (board), which postponed his parole release date for 24 months; the inmate filed a motion for leave to proceed.


OVERVIEW: The primary issue in this case was what constituted a "substantial question of law" for purposes of a motion for leave to proceed filed under Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.335(6), relating to judicial review of orders of the board. The inmate sought judicial review of a board order that postponed his parole release date for 24 months based on the board's determination that the inmate suffered from a present severe emotional disturbance that constituted a danger to the health or safety of the community. The inmate filed a motion for leave to proceed that raised two questions of law. The court allowed the motion for leave to proceed and concluded that a substantial question of law was presented by the inmate's motion for leave to proceed with judicial review. The inmate needed to identify a question of law that was more than seemingly plausible, but did not need to show that he would prevail on the question of law. The inmate's second question of law was a substantial one, as the inmate cited cases that appeared to support his position, but the legislature enacted legislation that arguably could be inconsistent with that case law.


OUTCOME: The court allowed the motion for leave to proceed.

 

581 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wigglesworth


A109998


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


186 Ore. App. 374; 63 P.3d 1185; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 255


March 28, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
February 19, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 990433162. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Jean Kerr Maurer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court of Multnomah County, Oregon, convicted defendant for failure to report as a sex offender, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 181.599, and denied defendant's motion for a judgment of acquittal. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by failing to determine whether his waiver of a jury trial was knowing and voluntary. Defendant argued that Or. Rev. Stat. § 181.597(1)(a) did not require him to notify the authorities when he moved out of state. The appellate court found that because the trial court received a written jury waiver from defendant, it was not required to inquire whether defendant's waiver was voluntary. Or. Rev. Stat. § 181.597(1)(a)(B) was clear: when a sex offender moved into Oregon, he or she had to notify the police within 10 days of a change of residence. Or. Rev. Stat. § 181.600 was not inconsistent with the conclusion that an Oregon resident sex offender who moved out of the state was required to report the move. Or. Rev. Stat. § 181.604 was consistent with § 181.597(1)(a) as imposing an obligation on a sex offender who was leaving the state to notify authorities within 10 days. Section 181.597(1)(a) was triggered by defendant's action in Oregon and had no extraterritorial effect. Defendant did not show that his right to free travel was burdened by the requirement that he notify the authorities of his change of residence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Thomas


A116172


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


186 Ore. App. 411; 63 P.3d 1242; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 265


November 13, 2002, Submitted on Record and Brief  
February 19, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 01-04-102-CR. Appeal from Circuit Court, Harney County. W. D. Cramer, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with committing first-degree animal abuse and unlawful use of a weapon, arising out of an incident in which he shot and killed a neighbor's dog. Defendant demurred to the first-degree animal abuse charge on the ground that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. The Harney County Circuit Court (Oregon) granted the demurrer on the ground that the statute was unconstitutionally overbroad. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found that Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.320 was unconstitutionally overbroad. The trial court held that under U.S. Const. amend. X, § 167.320 proscribed conduct which was constitutional. On appeal, the State argued that U.S. Const. amend. X did not speak to the issue of the authority of the State to regulate the conduct of its citizens. Instead, U.S. Const. amend. X spoke exclusively to the issue of the authority of the federal government in relation to the states and its citizens. The appellate court agreed with the State. At trial, defendant claimed that the right to kill animals such as rats, mice, and snakes was inalienable, but offered no explanation how the Oregon or federal Constitution protected that right. U.S. Const. amend. X did not protect against state infringement of an individual's right to kill mice, rats, rattlesnakes, gophers, or any other animals; it did not protect against state infringement of any right at all. Therefore, the trial court erred in granting the demurrer to the charge of first-degree animal abuse.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded.

 

583 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fleetwood


A116403


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


186 Ore. App. 305; 63 P.3d 42; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 160


October 31, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
February 12, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Fleetwood, 2003 Ore. LEXIS 811 (Or., Nov. 5, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Wasco County. John V. Kelly, Judge. CR92-167.
State v. Fleetwood, 331 Or. 511, 16 P.3d 503, 2000 Ore. LEXIS 986 (2000)



DISPOSITION: Trial court's order dismissing indictment reversed, and case remanded for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged in the Wasco County Circuit Court (Oregon) with distribution of a controlled substance. The court dismissed the charge with prejudice. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the trial court erred by dismissing the charge against defendant with prejudice. The court of appeals agreed. There was a delay of almost nine years between the date of the indictment and the date that proceedings were scheduled to resume in the trial court. The delay was due mainly to the appeal of a pre-trial motion to suppress. Defendant did not contend that the State's prosecution of the appeal or any action by the appellate courts was purposefully undertaken to "hamper the defense." Nor was the total time expended so "manifestly excessive" as to compel dismissal without any consideration of the justifications for delay or the prejudice, if any, to defendant. Although the total time expended on the interlocutory appeal was much greater than average, defendant made no showing of unjustified delay. Consequently, notwithstanding the very substantial length of pretrial delay, because the delay was justified and because defendant neither was incarcerated pending trial nor suffered any actual prejudice to his ability to present his defense, he was not entitled to dismissal. Therefore, the trial court erred.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for trial.

 

584 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hernandez


A111926


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


186 Ore. App. 86; 61 P.3d 951; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 96


July 31, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
January 29, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Umatilla County. Rudolph Murgo, Judge. CF990747.



DISPOSITION: Conviction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Circuit Court, Umatilla County (Oregon), which denied his motion for an acquittal and convicted him of supplying contraband, in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.185.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was incarcerated, was in possession of a prescription antidepressant without a prescription. He was then charged with supplying contraband. Defendant contended that the State did not prove its case, as it failed to establish that the medication endangered the security of the jail. After his motion for acquittal was denied, defendant sought review. In reversing, the court determined that the State was required to prove two prongs under Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.135(1)(a)(D). First, the State was required to prove that the item in question was prohibited by rule, statute, or order. Then, the State was required to show that its use endangered the safety or security of the institution or any person located therein. The court was required to give effect to all of § 162.135(a)(1)(D)'s provisions, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 174.010. Additionally, the court was bound by the rule of prior construction. Finally, the court held that the legislative history supported the two-prong interpretation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.135(a)(1)(D). Therefore, the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion for judgment of acquittal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction.

Drayton v. DOT


A113350 (Control), A113351, A113352 (Cases Consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


186 Ore. App. 1; 62 P.3d 430; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 98


July 2, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
January 29, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by, Vacated by, Remanded by Drayton v. DOT, 341 Ore. 244, 142 P.3d 72, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 1012 (2006)
Remanded by, in part Drayton v. DOT, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 1925 (Or. Ct. App., Dec. 13, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Review from Department of Transportation. 74385, 85901, 86367;.



DISPOSITION: Final orders in A113351 (Case No. 85901) and A113352 (Case No. 86367) reversed and remanded for reconsideration as to signs at milepoints 112.40, 112.42, and 112.46; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owner sought judicial review of three final orders by respondent Oregon Department of Transportation (department) requiring him to remove outdoor advertising signs that the department concluded did not comply with the Oregon Motorist Information Act (OMIA), Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 377.700 to 377.840.


OVERVIEW: The property owner had placed signs that were either along, or visible from, the highway. The Department issued three separate violations against the property owner. In the first case, the property owner had placed signs along the highway advertising a casino and welcoming drivers to the city; in the second case, the property owner had placed a sign along the highway advertising a restaurant; and in the third case the property owner had placed three signs that were visible from the highway, two related to his excavation business, and the third related to a business that was not on the property. The appellate court reversed the department's ruling on the signs that related to the excavation business and the casino as the department failed to provide the property owner of notice of any administrative rules in its notices. The court affirmed the ruling as to the sign that welcomed drivers, as while Or. Rev. Stat. § 377.735(1) exempted signs of a governmental unit, the property owner was not a governmental unit. The ruling regarding the sign advertising the restaurant was also affirmed as the sign advertised off-premises activity and was not exempt under Or. Rev. Stat. § 377.765(1).


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the department's rulings with respect to the signs where the department had not given the property owner notice of the administrative rules in its notice, but otherwise affirmed the department's rulings.

Lincoln Interagency Narcotics Team v. Kitzhaber


A115401


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


188 Ore. App. 526; 72 P.3d 967; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 875



January 27, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
July 9, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Lincoln Interagency Narcotics Team v. Kitzhaber, 336 Ore. 376, 84 P.3d 1080, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 186 (2004)
Review granted by Lincoln Interagency Narcots Team v. Kitzhaber, 336 Ore. 376, 84 P.3d 1080, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 187 (2004)
Reversed by Lincoln Interagency Narcotics Team v. Kitzhaber, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 999 (Or., Oct. 19, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 00C-19878. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Pamela L. Abernethy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a county and a law enforcement agency, challenged a decision from the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), which entered summary judgment in favor of defendant state officials in an action challenging the constitutionality of Ballot Measure 3 (2000) (Oregon Property Protection Act).


OVERVIEW: The county and the law enforcement agency contended that Measure 3 violated Or. Const. art. XVII, § 1 because it contained two or more constitutional amendments that should have been voted on separately. They also contended that Measure 3 addressed more than one subject, in violation of Or. Const. art. IV, § 1(2)(d). After a circuit court entered summary judgment in favor of the state officials, the county and the law enforcement agency sought review. In reversing, the court determined that Measure 3 was unconstitutional. The amendment to the Oregon Constitution made two or more substantive changes that were not closely related. Measure 3 added provisions to Or. Const. art. XV that prohibited the civil forfeiture of property unless the owner had been first convicted of a crime. Moreover, it directed how forfeited property should have been disposed of and distributed. The fact that the proposed provisions had a common objective did not mean that they were closely related. The provisions themselves affected the rights of different groups of people. The court did not consider the argument concerning Or. Const. art. IV, § 1(2)(d).


OUTCOME: The judgment of the circuit court was reversed.

Homebuilders Ass'n of Metro. Portland v. Tualatin Hills Park & Rec. Dist.


A111827


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


185 Ore. App. 729; 62 P.3d 404; 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 66


July 16, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
January 15, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] C99-0057CV. Appeal from Circuit Court, Washington County. Gayle A. Nachtigal, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, real estate developers and developers' association, sued defendant municipal district alleging that the district's resolution creating a system development charge did not comport with statutory provisions and that it violated a variety of state and federal constitutional guarantees. The Circuit Court, Washington County, Oregon, granted the district's partial motion for summary judgment. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district created a system development charge (SDC), which was a one time fee imposed on new developments to help offset the financial costs resulting from the growth associated with the new developments. Plaintiffs claimed that the SDC violated the Oregon Constitution as it constituted a regulatory taking of their property. Plaintiffs also claimed that the SDC violated U.S. Const. amend. V. The instant court rejected both claims. The court noted that no Oregon court had ever suggested that a legislatively imposed exaction of money applicable to a large group of people constituted a taking. Further, neither the SDC, or the methodology used to calculate it, violated U.S. Const. amend. V. as it met the reasonable relationship standard in that the charge was reasonably related to the district's stated goal of providing parks and recreational facilities.


OUTCOME: The grant of the district's partial summary judgment was affirmed.

State v. Makuch


A110292 (Control), A110839 (Cases Consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


185 Ore. App. 298; 59 P.3d 536; 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1966


August 8, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
December 11, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by State v. Makuch/Riesterer, 336 Ore. 92, 79 P.3d 313, 2003 Ore. LEXIS 770 (2003)
Affirmed by, Remanded by State v. Makuch, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 551 (Or., June 2, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Robert R. Selander, Judge. CR98-1962, CR98-1961.



DISPOSITION: Orders of suppression reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, the State appealed pretrial orders of the Clackamas County Circuit Court, Oregon, suppressing evidence of a marijuana grow operation in the basement of a business operated by defendants. The trial court concluded that Or. Rev. Stat. § 9.695(1) and (4) required suppression.


OVERVIEW: Police in the State of Washington conducted a search of the residence of defendants' attorney, including a search of the attorney's personal organizer, which disclosed defendants' names and addresses. Defendants argued that the evidence was obtained through exploitation of the unlawful search of the attorney's Washington residence. Although the State conceded that the police acted unlawfully under Washington law, it argued that that illegality was irrelevant to any issue in this case because defendants lacked a protected privacy interest in the attorney's residence or in his organizer. The instant court concluded that neither Or. Rev. Stat. § 9.695(1) and (4) nor Or. Const. art. I, § 9, compelled suppression. That information establishing the attorney's criminal involvement was obtained in violation of Washington law did not alter the fact that, at the time the officer read the organizer, he had "probable cause" for purposes of Or. Rev. Stat. § 9.695(2) to believe that the attorney had committed a crime. The organizer was not protected by Or. Rev. Stat. § 9.695(1). Consequently, the trial court erred in suppressing evidence gained as a result of the search of the organizer.


OUTCOME: The orders of suppression were reversed in part, and the case was remanded.

Powell v. Bunn


A108090


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


185 Ore. App. 334; 59 P.3d 559; 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1960


November 9, 2001, Argued and Submitted at Hood River High School, Hood River.  
December 11, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Powell v. Bunn, 336 Ore. 60, 77 P.3d 635, 2003 Ore. LEXIS 723 (2003)
Subsequent appeal at Powell v. Bunn, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 216 (Or. Ct. App., Mar. 2, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Joseph Ceniceros, Judge. 9805-03567.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the parent of a public school student, brought an action against respondents, the school district and its superintendent, in the Circuit Court, Multnomah County, Oregon, to challenge the district's policy of permitting the Boy Scouts to make in-school membership presentations to students. She contended the school district was violating the Establishment Clause. The circuit court rejected plaintiff's challenges. She appealed.


OVERVIEW: The parent of a public school child sought an injunction prohibiting the school district from continuing to allow the Boy Scouts to present membership information to students on public school premises. In response, the school superintendent conducted a preliminary investigation, after which he issued an order concluding there was no substantial basis to believe that the district was impermissibly involved with religious activity. The mother alleged her interests as a citizen-taxpayer gave her standing to seek judicial review of the order. The appellate court agreed that she had standing, but found no Establishment Clause violation. The court found that the Boy Scouts' conduct did not meet any of the prongs of the three-part Lemon test. The Boy Scout representatives who visited the school described the general kinds of activities involved in scouting but did not touch on anything having to do with religious beliefs or religious activities.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Boatman


A111380


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


185 Ore. App. 27; 57 P.3d 918; 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1758


July 31, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
November 13, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] C 00-03-32067. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Kimberly C. Frankel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court, Multnomah County, Oregon, convicted defendant for possession of a controlled substance, Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.992(1). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence after defendant consented to the search of his briefcase. Defendant contended that, under Or. Rev. Stat. § 807.570(4), he was unlawfully detained after the officer became 51 percent certain that defendant was how he said he was. The appellate court found that defendant failed to demonstrate that the deputy's subsequent request for consent extended the stop so as to give rise to a constitutional violation. The stop was validly ongoing at the time consent was requested. There was no evidence that, at the time the deputy requested consent, the officer had completed and issued the traffic citation or documentation associated with the tow and impoundment. Rather, all reasonable record inferences were to the contrary. Defendant did not show that the request for consent occurred beyond the time when those tasks should reasonably have been completed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Safeport, Inc. v. Equip. Roundup & Mfg.


A112787


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


184 Ore. App. 690; 60 P.3d 1076; 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1728


August 1, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
November 6, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Safeport, Inc. v. Equip. Roundup & Mfg., 2003 Ore. LEXIS 159 (Or., Mar. 4, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 98C-10775. Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Joseph V. Ochoa, Judge.



DISPOSITION: On appeal, affirmed in part and reversed in part and remanded for entry of award of attorney fees. On cross-appeal, reversed and remanded for jury trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation brought an action against defendant contractor for, inter alia, breach of contract, when the contractor stopped working on a construction project. The contractor counterclaimed based on the amount unpaid on the contract and brought a third-party claim against defendant property owner and defendant bank. The Circuit Court, Marion County, Oregon, granted the contractor summary judgment. The parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the contractor argued that the trial court erred in denying it attorney fees as it was not a plaintiff or cross-complainant within the meaning of Or. Rev. Stat. § 87.057. The appellate court determined that even if § 87.057 did apply, the contractor proved compliance with its requirements, and the trial court erred in denying the contractor's motion to amend its pleading to conform to that evidence. Further, the corporation failed to identify how it would be prejudiced if the amendment were allowed. Because the contractor failed to provide the bank with notice of its right to a lien, the contractor was not entitled to priority for materials charges and for labor charges that were lumped together with materials charges. As there was no evidence that the contractor would be unable to satisfy its entire judgment from the foreclosed property, the record supported the trial court's determination that the property owner had not been unjustly enriched. The appellate court interpreted Or. Rev. Stat. § 87.060(3) to mean the trial court's authority to resolve pleaded issues was limited to issues subsumed within the lien claim.


OUTCOME: The portion of the judgment denying the contractor attorney fees was reversed and remanded; the portion of the judgment denying the corporation's request for a jury trial on its breach of contract claim was reversed and remanded. Otherwise, the judgment was affirmed.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Malheur County. Rodney W. Miller, Judge. A00080111 and A00080112.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the operators of nude dance clubs, appealed from the order of the Circuit Court, Malheur County, Oregon, upholding their convictions for violating Nyssa City, Or., Code § 5.10.130(17).


OVERVIEW: The City of Nyssa, Oregon, enacted an ordinance that requires nude dancers to remain at least four feet away from the audience. Weber v. Oakridge Sch. Dist. 76

Moran v. Weldon (In re Moran)


A116453


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


184 Ore. App. 269; 57 P.3d 898; 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1627


July 3, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
October 16, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 0100008A. Appeal from Circuit Court, Wasco County. John V. Kelly, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father appealed a judgment of the Wasco County Circuit Court, Oregon, granting the petition of respondents, the brother and sister-in-law of the children's mother, to adopt the children without the father's consent.


OVERVIEW: The father, who had been serving a sentence of more than three years' imprisonment on a criminal conviction, argued that Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.322 was unconstitutional. The instant court did not reach the constitutional issues, but it construed the statute to require proof of a basis for terminating the father's parental rights in addition to the fact of his imprisonment. The instant court observed that the trial court did not believe that it was necessary to prove any of the statutory criteria for terminating the father's parental rights or to show that the father had done anything that placed the child at a risk of harm, that demonstrated an inability to be an adequate parent, or that constituted parental misconduct. Under its construction of the statute, the instant court gave effect to the procedures that the statute prescribed without permitting the termination of a parent's rights in the absence of evidence that would have otherwise supported that action, thereby protecting a parent's fundamental constitutional right while preserving the statutory scheme that the legislature created. The trial court did not consider the issues essential to an adoption under § 109.322.


OUTCOME: The judgment of adoption was reversed, and the case was remanded.

Wilson & Wilson


A113524


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


184 Ore. App. 212; 55 P.3d 1106; 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1563


July 9, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
October 9, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by, Vacated by, Remanded by Wilson & Wilson, 337 Ore. 327, 99 P.3d 290, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 598 (2004)
On remand at, Remanded by Wilson & Wilson, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 482 (Or. Ct. App., Apr. 20, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 9908-67658 and 0004-63675. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Keith Meisenheimer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Award of custody reversed and remanded; attorney fee award vacated and remanded for reconsideration; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a dissolution case, the Circuit Court, Multnomah County, Oregon, awarded the custody of two children to respondent, the husband. The older child was the biological daughter of appellant, the wife, and the stepchild of the husband. The younger child was the biological daughter of the husband and the wife. The circuit court also awarded the husband $ 10,000 for attorney fees. The wife appealed from the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The husband and the wife were married in September 1992. At that time, the wife already had a 22-month-old daughter, C, from an earlier relationship. The husband and the wife were more or less equally involved in nurturing and caring for C. The husband was the only father figure C knew. C used the husband's last name and learned that he was not her biological father only after the husband and the wife separated. Another daughter, E, was born to the parties in July 1995. After E's birth, the wife took a job working nights and weekends, and the husband became jealous. Ultimately, the couple divorced. The husband was given custody of the children. The court of appeals held that the custody of C was determined under Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.119(2)(b) (1997). Although the statute was not unconstitutional, the trial court applied it unconstitutionally, because the trial court used the "best interest" standard instead of the "parental preference" standard. Applying the parental preference standard, the wife was to have sole custody of C. In turn, under the "best interest" standard for determining custody disputes between two legal parents, the wife was also to have sole custody of E.


OUTCOME: The award of custody was reversed and remanded; the attorney fee award was vacated and remanded for reconsideration; otherwise, the judgment was affirmed.

State v. Ehret


A111249


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


184 Ore. App. 14; 55 P.3d 518; 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1552


June 28, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
October 2, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Ehret, 37 Or. 84, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 412 (2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] F04666. Appeal from Circuit Court, Union County. Phillip A. Mediguren, Judge.
State v. Ehret, 184 Or. App. 1, 55 P.3d 512, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1545 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's motion to suppress statements that he made to the police, and the evidence seized from him and from his father's car during a traffic stop was denied and defendant was convicted for possession of a controlled substance in the Circuit Court of Union County (Oregon). Defendant appealed, asserting that under Or. Const. art. I, § 9, and U.S. Const. amend. IV, the evidence should have been suppressed.


OVERVIEW: An officer stopped defendant's father's car, in which defendant was a passenger. The father told the officer that marijuana might be in the car. The officer then confronted defendant, telling defendant that a drug dog might be brought in, and defendant pulled several baggies containing marijuana from his pocket. More drugs were then found in defendant's backpacks in the car. Defendant argued that the evidence was seized as the result of the unlawful detention of the vehicle, and his father's person, and it therefore was subject to suppression. The State countered that, because defendant did not assert a violation of his own personal rights, he could not bring a constitutional claim. The appellate court agreed with the State. Any incidental detention of defendant as a passenger during the time of a lawful stop of the driver did not implicate defendant's Or. Const. art. I, § 9 rights. Further, defendant produced the drugs voluntarily. Although the police traded on a prior illegality (the detention of the father), in the absence of a constitutional violation of defendant's personal rights, that occurred before his voluntary production of the drugs, there was no ground for suppression.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed.

 

599 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Meyer


A113345


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


183 Ore. App. 536; 53 P.3d 940; 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1401


January 3, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
September 11, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] DO77898. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Harold J. Blank, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of violating the basic speed rule in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 811.100. The Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon) found that defendant's stop, which occurred in the State of Washington, was authorized under Or. Rev. Stat. § 810.410(2). Defendant challenged the trial court's ruling.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was observed speeding in Oregon by a police officer. Because defendant was traveling on a bridge in a construction zone, the officer did not activate his overhead lights for safety reasons until defendant had crossed the bridge and crossed into Washington. Defendant was cited for violating the basic speed rule. He filed a motion to dismiss the case on the ground that the officer did not stop him or cite him until after defendant was in Washington. The trial court found that the stop was authorized, but the court disagreed and reversed on appeal. The court held that the district court erred in denying defendant's motion to dismiss. After reviewing the legislative history, the court held that § 810.410(2) provided that, at most, an officer was authorized to issue traffic violation citations in a state outside of Oregon, but only if that action was authorized by the other state. Under Wash. Rev. Code § 10.89.010, an officer from another state was only entitled to pursue a person into Washington if that person was believed to have committed a felony or was driving recklessly. This was not the case here, and thus the officer had no authority to stop defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's ruling.

 

600 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Williamson v. Hunt


A112192


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


183 Ore. App. 339; 51 P.3d 694; 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1265


May 20, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
August 14, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 99-DR-0887. Appeal from Circuit Court, Josephine County. J. Loyd O'Neal, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents challenged a decision of the Circuit Court, Josephine County (Oregon), which awarded respondent grandmother the right to visit the parents' child despite their objection. The parents argued that the trial court's decision violated Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.121 (repealed 2001), and if not, that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The child originally spent a fair amount of time with the grandmother. When the parents became dissatisfied with the grandmother's conduct toward the child, which included giving her snacks and presents, the parents began to limit visitation. The grandmother filed this action to compel visitation. The trial court ruled in favor of the grandmother, but the court reversed on appeal. The court first found that Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.121 (repealed 2001) and the purportedly retroactive replacement of it with Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.119 (2001) did not affect the action because the petition was filed under the 1997 edition of the statute, and the retroactivity section applied only to petitions filed under the 1999 edition of the statute. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutional because it required the trial court's application of the best interest test only as that test was subject to and limited by the fundamental rights of the parents. In this case, the court found that the parents' concerns were not sufficiently weighted, and the grandmother did not overcome the presumption that the parents' decision to limit or ban visitation was in the child's best interest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's award of visitation to the grandmother.
